RATING: 8.34
There are a lot of paths to victory that a player can explore, and every route you take is adventurous. You can try to focus on one strategy, or you can mix and match, but you can never do everything you want, and that's the beauty of it that will draw you to play it again. Variable setup introduces great replay value. The downside is that you have to play it multiple times to fully grasp the game since it is not as straightforward as it seems once you are familiar with the game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Quick playing game that's intuitive but has depth about running cattle from Texas to Kansas City (and beyond).  

The game is variable in length, scales well from 2-4 players, and has fast turns. Each turn you move, and it's point to point movement from building to building (thus movement typically slows down later in the game when more buildings are out), and then utilize a buildings power(s). There are neutral buildings, which are laid out at the start of the game and provide significant variability depending on the order that they come out, and personal buildings which first need to be built and can only be used by their owner. You start with a hand of 4 cattle cards (all players have the same starting deck but due to shuffling/drawing and deck building and thinning, hands will vary) and many building actions allow for card play. You always refill your hand at the end of your turn. 

Your goal is to score the most points by game end and there are several ways to score (point salad): 1) buying new cow cards, 2) building personal buildings, 3) making cattle deliveries, 4) scoring end game objective cards, 5) clearing hazards, 6) building train stations, 7) having a large supply of assistants (cowboys, builders, engineers), 8) some other minor ways such as cash in hand, etc. You are following a trail to Kansas City which has branch points but eventually you will reach your destination at which point you must make a delivery. This typically has several benefits: you get cash in hand (very useful), you place a disk on a station (can provide end game points) and the disk that was placed comes from your personal action board which opens up improved actions. This upgrade effect comes in two flavours either improved actions such as more movement or larger hand size, or improved auxiliary actions; auxiliary actions are minor but important actions you can take at certain buildings or at any movement endpoint instead of doing a building action. 

Each delivery involves seeding a track of hired personalities which serves as a supply to buy from but which is also a game clock; the more frequent the deliveries, the faster the game. Personalities play a significant role as they boost your abilities in three key areas: 1) cowboys allow for cheaper prices when buying cattle cards, 2) builders allow for more powerful and higher point value buildings, and 3) engineers improve the movement of your train (you want to move your train as it reduces the freight costs of delivering cattle to far away destinations and it opens up train station building opportunities). As well personalities provide one off bonuses if you collect enough of the same type and score end game points if you acquire enough of them. 

This is a fun easy to play gamers game with significant variability and is a great follow up to Pfisters' previous design Mombasa.  Looking forward to more releases from this designer.  Own the [boardgame=225704]Promo Station Master tiles[/boardgame] and the [boardgame=238620]Eleventh Building tile[/boardgame] promo.

RATING: 7
Undoubtedly a great game, but not likely to become one of my favorites. The good: multiple viable strategies, theme works nicely with the game, a fun acceleration toward the end of the game as everyone rushes to sell. The cons: hard to care about what other players are doing (not that this is unusual for a euro), you can see who the winner will be many turns before the end, complex symbology makes it tough to teach to new players & opens up opportunities for misunderstandings although the game itself is really not very complex.  I have no doubt that this will be someone's favorite game, and I'd play it again, but I don't think I'll want to own it.

RATING: 8
7 plays / 2,3,4 players (4p = 3.5h)

LIKE: Mild interaction, good strategy. It's possible to mess with your opponents by adding obstacles in the worker paths. Lots to think about, balancing ways to get an engine going. The worker buildings, where to place them, how to maneuver your workers each pass, was simply brilliant.

DISLIKE: Lots of rules, some easy to forget. Theme is not attached to the mechanisms, especially deck building with cows. I felt the deck building/shuffling was a little overkill, and introduces a random element.

OVERALL: Very good game. It's a point salad and a mechanism salad, but it works and I like it. Compared to Mombasa (Pfister's other design), they are equally fun, but I like the theme a tad better in this one (cow cards vs. pith helmets).

RATING: 7
There seems to be multiple ways to victory in Great Western Trail, none of which feels overly dominant which I take as a sing of a good game. Every action seems significant although not decisive. The only things that seem like a must, are getting those extra hand cards as early as possible and increasing your movement speed early enough so that you can avoid being caught in the web of buildings other players are laying down.

I must admit that the game can feel a bit repetitive since the only thing that changes is that you get more and more money every round from shipping your cattle. Luckily the game progresses at a decent pace so that it does not become too tedious.

RATING: 6
1st play: Excellent blend of route building (sortof), deck building, and engine building. Approaching each game you can focus on your deck, your workers, or your engineering, but it's hard to do all things at once. Deck building for cows is really unique and fits the theme well. The game runs a little long, but I'm not exactly sure how it would be shortened. The symbols are good and we didn't have to refer to the rules at all in our play which is a great sign. Nice to see a western themed game without high-noon shootouts.

2nd play: Great second play, I managed to get the flow of the game and placed my buildings a lot better to synch with my strategy. The goals each game are roughly the same, so I'm not sure how much variability there is. There's an interesting optimization goal with each play, but it would be easy to see more buildings in an expansion.

RATING: 10
I already enjoyed learning other game from Alexander Pfister this year, Mombasa, and I though I would like this almost same complexity game. And I liked it even more than Mombasa, the reason is that I think things are more organic in this game, the interaction between players and between the lots of stuff you need to take care and the correlation between them.

It is a brilliant design that have a lot of cool mechanics into a very cool game. And the theme, in spite of being pasted, makes even more sense than Mombasa does.

Another thing that I liked is that there are a lot of paths for victory, a lot of strategies and a feeling that you are still playing and giving your best even when knowing you cannot win anymore, that feeling is great for a game that is supposed to be fun for everyone.

Highly recommended.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Unpleasantly surprised with this game, though perhaps playing with four is peculiarly bad?   First, did I feel like I was thematically doing anything other than playing around with tired euro mechanics over and over again?  Nope.  And no way was I driving a herd of cattle, as the game was trying to invite me to believe.  

Instead, I felt like I was drawing cards, again and again, moving a few spaces on a board, and buying cards to build a deck, or buying buildings to put on the board, or doing things that gave me victory points, or made it easier for me to earn victory points.  I was not interacting at all with the other players, and as such, the gaming experience was dry and isolating.  Are we even in the same room playing a game?  Boring!  Then again, I tend to find this to be a problem in a lot of deck building games.

This game evidently represents a trend in euro design that simply puts together interlocking basic or standard euro mechanics, with little attention to theme, and entices euro gamers into trying to optimize the distinctive tangle of this particular euro mechanics collection.  If the mechanics are fresh, fun, interactive, it can work for me.  Here, the tangle felt stale, dull and isolating, not to mention tenuously themed.

In general, I am no longer a fan of games where you move round and round a board, again and again, over the course of several hours (sorry Monopoly and Parcheesi, my childhood friends).  I think I can tolerate this mechanic if the game is short, but here, the circular pony ride felt long, too long pardner, interminably long.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Great Western Trail is somewhat of a disappointing game. Luck of the draw and tokens can play a big part in gameplay, way bigger than it should in game of this weight and length. The theme is really not there at all, it's a mix match of different mechanism and scoring is a point salad of many mostly unrelated things. I also fail to see the fun in the samey rinse and repeat travel that should be the thematic core of the game. The art is bland (cow art is nice though), but components are of good quality. Replayability is good because the map, cards and tokens are never the same, but it's hard to see this one returning to the table too often because of the lack of fun and bad luck/weight ratio.

Fun factor: 2/5
Strategy vs. luck: 3/5
Mechanics/gameplay tied to the theme: 2/5
Component/art quality: 3/5
Replayability: 4/5

RATING: 8

The game itself is a neat little loop engine, where you're going through both the board and your deck over and over while attempting to make each pass better for you. Overall, I liked it. Would recommend having someone teach it. Would also note that me and one of those other new players later RIPPED thru a 2P game in like 45m.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Un juego excelente!!

Reglas no complicadas una vez están digeridas y aprehendidas. Y sobre todo diversión sin grandes dosis de puteo. múltiples posibilidades de conseguir puntuaciones y rápido de jugar.
Cada partida es distinta a la anterior gracias a la combinación de dos cuestiones:

- las edificaciones predefinidos del tableo se colocan aleatoriamente.
- los edificios de los jugadores solo se pueden construir por una de las dos caras aleatoriaz que se selecciona al principio de la partida.

Cada partida, gracias a la combinación de estas dos aleatoridades es dferente y por eso no hay una estrategia definida que funcione con todas las combinaciones.  

Mezcla de creación de mazo, construcción de edificios, creación de combos... puteo indircto alrestode jugadores y turnos cortos que agradeceb sacarlo a mesa.

La temática es accesoria en los euros pero de tan amable que es, con nuevas vaquitas, granjeros,y edificaciones, trenes de por medio, track de puntuación con jefes de estación que bonifican, en fin, que es todo tan bonito que disfruto creando edifciios, contaratando trabajadores o avanzando mi tren por EEUU. de verdad que es de los pocos euros que me meten el tema.

Toda una sorpresa del 2016 que gracias a Carlos y a Coco conozco y difruto. Lo menos han caído 15 partidas y sigo queriendo volverlo a jugar. me faltan maneras de ver la victoria!!!!!

RATING: 8
Ritka, hogy ennyire szarul megy egy játék (2 parti alapján írom) és mégis ennyire tetszik. Nagyon jól össze van rakva a szabály (vagy csak nagyon jól mondták, mert én nem olvastam), kevés kérdés merült fel közben, ráadásul a bonyolultsági szinthez képest gyorsan el is mondható. A játék nagyon sokutasnak tűnt elsőre, de azért vannak dolgok, amikre nagy eséllyel rá kell menni, a mi két partink alapján a pakli karcsúsítása mindenképp ilyennek tűnik. Nagyon tetszik, hogy magunknak ütemezhetjük a játék ritmusát. Végigrohanhatunk a pályán, de akár megállhatunk minden akciónál, függetlenül attól, a többiek hogy tesznek. Ez persze a játék hosszára is kihat, szerintem ha mindenki keveset lép, akár többszöröse is lehet a játékidő (és a pontszámok). Nekünk meglepően gyorsan lementek a partik, 3 fővel volt 3 ill. 2 óra. Nekem túl rövid is volt a 2 órás, nem tudtam kibontakozni:) A vége nagyon izgalmas, amikor már látszik, hogy nagyjából 3-4 akciónk van, de pontosan még nem tudjuk, mennyi. Persze ha az ember rosszul alapozta meg a játékát, akkor a vége szenvedős. A végén egyik partiban már csak bekkeltem, hogy véletlenül se érjek körbe, mert ott várt a minusz pont. Apropó minusz pont: volt egyszer, hogy nüanszon múlt, hogy 12 helyett csak 11 érték jött össze a kör végére, ezen 11 pontot buktam, ezt soknak éreztem, de ez legyen az én bajom. Túl gyorsan rohangáltam körbe, és nem volt elég jó a paklim. Utólag ez kamikaze taktikának bizonyult:) A játék szerencsére hülyebiztos, mert a -6 pont mellé ad 6 pénzt, amivel elindulhatunk a következő körben.
Összességében egy nagyon jó játékról beszélünk, bár nekem nem top10 szint azért, utána azt mondtam, hogy nekem ez Marco Polo szint volt. Nem is túl hosszú, szóval aki a középnehéz eurókat szereti, az feltétlen próbálja ki. Bár a cím (főleg a magyar) kicsit megtévesztő, de én még tematikát is kiéreztem belőle, és küllemre is tetszett. Összességében bármikor katagória, csak azért nem veszem meg, mert itthon mostanában esélytelennek érzem. Bár mint megtudtam, már ennek is van szóló variánsa.

RATING: 8
Mr. Alexander Pfister is definitely the most inspired games designer of these last few years: with Great Western Trail he scores another sound hit, after the lighter Isle of a Skye and the similarly heavy Mombasa.

GWT is cumbersome to teach, but surprisingly smooth to play: after a couple of turns in the first game, everything falls into place. But it will disclose its secrets and delights, in form of nuances and strategies, well after many plays.
Here, the deckbuilding aspect is just a gear among many others, in a jaw dropping clockwork with many moving parts, many different approaches and many ways to subtly influence other players...

It was a while since I've been so impressed by a german game. This one deserves an 8, but the rating will possibly go up after some more plays.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Initial Impression: I usually enjoy games more when they convey a strong sense of theme and the Western theme in particular is a personal favorite of mine. To my surprise, this game's theme didn't really shine through for me. However, I found that this didn't bother me so much, as the gameplay was fun. With the many paths to victory, do you build lots of buildings? Do you drive as many cattle as quickly as you can to the market? Do you concentrate on train stations? There's a definite sense that you need to do everything but simply don't have enough time in which to do it. Player interaction was fairly minimal but I actually didn't miss it. When the game was over, I immediately had the desire to play again in order to explore different options. This is the mark of a great game.

RATING: 9
DUBLIN
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Great Western Trail (GWT) is a mash-up of a number of mechanisms. It doesn't feel as smoothly mixed as some others that I rate highly, such as Troyes and Ginkgopolis. However, this game provides more grand strategy than those games. 

I found that the first few plays of GWT felt more ponderous than Brass. That's largely due to the amount of iconography and lack of player aids (which I've since printed off BGG for use by new players). Once this has been played enough to internalize the icons, this plays quite quickly. GWT's setup and chit draws provide a welcome dose of variability from play to play. The initial difficulty in getting used to GWT is outweighed by what it becomes: a fast playing, deep strategy game that plays well at 2-4 and that requires some tactical play to succeed. 

Another of GWT's strengths is that it allows the players to choose the pace of the game. Want a slow game? Take your time to Kansas City. Want a faster game? Rush to Kansas City. One player rushing to Kansas City? Throw a bunch of buildings on the trail. Players trying to slow you down? Increase your movement or throw down a few specific buildings that give you free movement.

I do wish there was a bit more connection between its theme and its mechanisms. Other euro-style games I've rated highly, such as Vinhos and Troyes, feel more thematic. I think the disconnect is kind of due to the inaccurate representation of the true "Great Western Trail" that the game puts in front of you. Non-luck purists might be turned off to the "luck of the draw" in this game, but there are mechanisms to combat that aspect and that is one of the ways in which the game builds inefficiencies into a player's default engine.

RATING: 9
UPDATE: [1.15.17]
I got to log my first "nearly full" play of Great Western Trail.
2P game.

Has anyone else played GWT?
Thoughts?

Sarah (my wife) and I enjoyed it quite a bit.  
The game turn is simple:

1) Move your cowdude up to his allotted number of spaces.
2) Perform any actions permitted at the building/outpost where you have landed.
3) Make sure your "hand" (the cattle you are driving to Kansas City is back to the "herd" limit)

Things to manage in the game (which makes for the interest and fun)

1) Type of cattle you are bringing to Kansas City.  Only unique cattle will be sold!  So off loading duplicate cows on the "trail" and picking up new ones via outposts, or the cattle market is one set of variables that needs to be managed.  Round em' up.  Head 'em out -- RAWHIDE!

2) Moving your train.  Once cattle have been delivered to Kansas City, you may deliver the cattle along your railway.  The farther your locomotive is down the track, the further West you can deliver your cattle (and also NOT have to pay for intervening whistle stops that eat up your profit just made by selling the cattle).  Also beware, if you deliver cattle to two "neighboring" cities, you will have to take the penalty or bonus that lies in between the two cities (e.g. -3 VP's or -1 VP and draw an objective card, etc.)

3) Building and placing buildings/outposts along the trail.  Newly placed buildings only help the player who placed the building, if they land on it in a subsequent turn.  However, opposing players may have to pay a "tax" for passing through or landing on the hostile building.  So there is a progressive set of "toll booths" that can be set up along the trail.  Understanding where to place these building for your max. benefit and your opponents max. pain is another dimension this game offers.  Build wisely.

4) Managing your money.  Players need to understand how much money the will likely need to fully maximize their effectiveness as they move from Texas to Kansas City.  Being a dollar short here or there may lead to diminished returns anywhere along the trail.  

5) Hiring workers.  There are 3 types.  Cowboys (help get you discounts at the cattle market); Conductors (help you gain bonuses with your locomotive); and Craftsman (help you build better buildings).  Knowing how much to spend on which worker, and how many of each type is something your ranch's HR dept. needs to figure out in order to be effective.

There is a ton to explore in this game, and I'm sure it only gets more cut throat when the player count goes up.




[1.12.17] The mechanical elegance in game play reminds me of Puerto Rico.
Game play is super simple, but the strategic depth is enamoring.

RATING: 8.5
First Play: Had rules explained and it came across as considerably more involved that I'd anticipated. Certainly more involved and fiddly than Mombasa. This game borrows very heavily from Caylus and Russian Railroads. I'm surprised that most reviewers so far haven't felt the need to mention this. Those well versed in deck builder games will have a head start. First game is a big step to file and sort the icons. Need to play again but at this stage not as impressed as I was with Mombasa.

RATING: 7.5
A hodge-podge of Euro mechanics that blend pretty nicely. There is technically a theme here that you're a cattle driver trying to deliver cattle to Kansas City and get them on trains. You have a hand of cards that represent your cattle and along the trail you are making stops trying to make money/get more cattle/hire workers/etc. None of it really fits thematically as it's likely most of the cattle you end up delivering weren't even the cattle you started with. The timer/worker track is clunky and very fiddly. Starting player order feels important and the randomness of the deck draws can be a not-insignificant factor in what is a 2+ hour game with 4p. There's three general point-generating strategies that correspond to the worker types (buying cows, buying buildings, and train-route points). It feels like the building strategy really just supports the other two and isn't viable on it's own but I could be wrong. In the end, if you get past the lack of theme and other minor negatives, this is still a very solid Euro game that gets my brain thinking pretty hard. This isn't a game I need to own myself but I'm happy to play if given the opportunity. 

Update (8/2017): 7 => 7.5
A minor bump up after my third play. We weren't playing a couple rules correctly in my first two plays, one of which was people can start anywhere on the trail they want and don't have to start at the beginning. This definitely helps the starting player order problem I had. I also just know at this point I am not very good at the game which I know isn't helping my opinion of it. =) This game is definitely a quality game and I can see why other people love it but it doesn't soar as high for me. My 7.5 rating plants this game firmly in the realm of "I probably won't suggest it but would happily play it given the chance."

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
It is interesting the way that this game has developed in our game group. 

We initially thought that the "cowboy" strategy was very strong, but it has been proven with subsequent games that going with engineers and builders is just as strong. There is no broken or "one way to go" strategy as far as we have experienced and the game seems very well balanced. 

There are a lot of nuances and subtleties going on in this game that the first few games don't disclose. The pace that you maintain, the obstacles put forth to other players via buildings/hazards and just trying different means to get your economy going are some of the things that keep you engaged and entertained. 

Replayability galore!

If you have a heavier game group that enjoys the challenge of figuring out different opaque avenues for victory; you can't go wrong with this title.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Great Western Trail is a game where you herd your cattle from Texas to Kansas City and deliver them by train out west. Now, if that theme doesn't get you excited about a board game, I don't know what will. Seriously, it is a theme I haven't seen before and I've always been way more interested in game play and how the game captures whatever theme is used than just the theme itself. This game is a massive cornucopia of mechanics. There is deck-building, hand management, building (tile placement), movement, special abilities and planning for end game goals. The rules take quite a bit to get through and to absorb, but like just about every game I play... it was easy to get once we were through a few rounds. At first, it felt like too much of a mish-mash and kind of a mess to make sense of. The more we played, the clearer it became and I jave a feel for how the game works and different ways to score points. I look forward to playing this one again.

RATING: 9
Update: After 6 games, bumping from 8-->9. After the first couple games, certain tracks seem OP (cowboys/cows), but on repeated play, the variety of options and subtle player interaction really shine. Could maybe be a 10 if I could get 4 players to play in the same time it normally takes to play 3p.
---
What's better than Choose Your Own Adventure? Choose Your Own Rondel! Interesting take on worker placement, in that it's a roll and move minus the roll. And with forks in the path, you choose how you move through the path, with each space offering stuff to do. In general you can plan a move or two ahead, and the spaces are unique enough that each space where you could stop is an important decision for whether to stop or not. There's tension in that generally all the main spots are good (except spots built by other players), but you also want to generally move briskly to cycle the circuit as often as possible. By cycling too fast without accomplishing much creates its own problems, because every time you cycle, you need to sell cows and score them, and with a fixed number of scoring options that each player can only use once each, you can only sell a low-value set of cattle a couple times before it becomes a big problem. As any good euro, there are multiple axes of progress, and you want to develop them all, but can never do as much as you want. Oh, and tell your xx friends it's a train game! Sort of.

First play: Seemed interesting, but concerns over both the deck-building in a longish game, and the length itself. It's one of those games that despite concerns, I found myself thinking about after playing it. Both issues faded on 2nd play. I'm now looking forward to more.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
What is it that draws hordes of gamers to this type / style of game? That was the question my gaming friend and I asked ourselves after having wrestled through the rule book plus a quick verbal explanation, and a number of sample rounds. Our answer was inconclusive at best. Gamers simply [i]do[/i], for unfathomable reasons.

Well, not entirely unfathomable. Gamers tend to want 'lots of stuff', and they get it by the herdload in GWT. A gazillion little actions await the brave cowboys who begin driving ever growing cattle herds—a little bit in deckbuilding / deckthinning-style—towards Kansas City. Of course any charm of the subject matter is lost in this icon tornado. Not that it bothers me personally as the subject is the first thing out the window whenever I play a game, but still. If you want to, you can easily lose yourself in layer upon layer of deliberations; and there will be very little cowboy charm to these. 

In my short, mangled experience three things stood out as particularly irksome. One is that the game does not keep a running score. You play, and play, and play... and only at the end will you find out how much VPs your strategy netted you and the others, exactly. This is inexcusable in a game of this length. There's bound to be the usual defense that it prevents AP and hitting on the leader, as if those are Bad Things which need to be avoided. It is a game, there is competition, and someone will win. Competition is difficult, and annoying, but it leads to memorable sessions. It tells me something else about the population of gamers who pushed this title into the upper regions of BGG's ranking list, flawed as that is: namely that they don't really mind playing for its own sake, and not to win. It probably isn't hard to keep a running score (or an approximation of it) but the fact that this isn't included in the core rules is 'no'.

The second is that, obviously, gamers are turned into little optimising human computers. The is a tonne of little rules to absorb; eleven (!) sources of VP to take into consideration (granted, not all of them are equally important); optimising substrategy A vs. B vs. C vs. ... . Whatever happened to lean & mean? This game rewards playing more often. Clearly so. Why not do away with half to three quarters of this convoluted mess and end up with a title which too rewards playing more often, but then in a nimble and responsive setting, taking up far less table space to boot? It doesn't make any sense. Unless of course today's crop of gamers equate 'game quality' with 'lots of bits' in which case GWT strikes another home run. 

The third is simply that the artwork is hopelessly overburdened to convey all the information to the players. Iconography is small and tiny, and not particularly friendly on the eyes while reading upside down. If the response now is 'well, you don't really have to read it all upside down', then I can equally wonder what the point of playing this grotesque monster is.

This game is not something I would have chosen to play based on a cursory study of the rules; I really wanted to pack up before the explanation was even halfway. As a result I'm not going to rate this title, but I'm fairly certain it would end up with a 3 at best, and most likely a 2.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
This game is so pleasant to play, you feel very satisfied when progressing through Texas and ride your herd of cows to Kansas City. There is a lot to do, yet you are not overwhelmed with choices as your actions per turn are very simple - you just progress step by step to reach the goal.

What I like:
- Theme. It is stronger there than in most euro games and you really feel like the cowboy taking care of your cows. Also the mechanics in the game are well implemented to match the theme. 
- Game uses several mechanics which are interesting, fun and easy to grasp. There is your own deck of cards - your herd of cows (deckbuilding), there is also interesting rondel mechanics - the journey to the Kansas - which you progress with your pawn (point to point movement), you can build buildings to the strategic places on the track (tile placement), you try to sell as valuable herd as you can (hand management), and there is also the train track where you can build stations and hire station masters. You can also hire your own workers (cowboys, engineers and builders) which can help you whatever strategy you choose.
- You feel close to be available to do anything you want. There a lot of ways to choose, the sandbox feeling is quite there.
- Production quality is nice. It is nice look to the board and every game piece is well manufactured. The special mention goes to the game board, which is not only nice, but also well designed and it holds a lot of details.
- Although there is a lot to do, rules are very clear and you don't need to refer a rulebook after the first gamethrough. Buildings had a very understandable symbols and the rules are implemented elegantly. The rulebook is very well written - I would say it is one of the best rulebooks I have seen so far.
- There is also unofficial solo mode using automa. Very challenging but very welcomed.

What I don't like:
- my only complaint about production quality is player board. It should be made of a thicker paper at least.
- This game requires all the player to have similar skill level, there is almost no way the weaker player to beat the stronger one. There is also no catch up mechanism (at least I am not aware of anything), although it is hard to say who is winning because this is at the end the point salad game where a lot of things are counted.
- sometimes you are just very unlucky in your card draw which can lead to the weaker delivery, which hurts mostly in first three deliveries - although there are lot of ways how to build your hand during the journey. But sometimes you didn't get good hand no matter how hard you try.
- there is a lot of stuff to set up and tear down. Unless you have everything well organized, it could be pain to do it. But it is understandable as this is heavier euro.

RATING: 4
Initial impression was not good.

There's simply too much going on in this game, and most of it will be irrelevant depending on what strategy you pick.  You still have to fiddle with those bits though.  Very inelegant, despite its "simple" turn structure.

I'm worried that this will end up near two games that I really didn't enjoy: The Gallerist and Russian Railroads.  The Gallerist had the same "simple" structure: pick one of four spots, do the consequences.  But you really had to plan everything out in advance, and not just in the "make sure you have enough wood and reed to build a room" kind of way.  You had to see how all of the gears interlocked to give you what you need to carry out your convoluted plan.  And if you didn't line it up right?  The gears ground to a halt, and your plans sputtered and flailed as you ran out of money and failed to do anything of note.  Same for GWT?

Russian Railroads I disliked because it was about picking one strategy and just doing that thing over and over and over to its inevitable conclusion.  No variance outside of minor trivialities, and just a quest to be ever so slightly more efficient than the other guy.  Sure, maybe two expert players could see one minor slip-up and exploit it to win the game, but I didn't have the patience or interest to invest that far.  Same for GWT?

I didn't feel like I was building anything.  I wasn't making interesting decisions from a limited selection of cards.  It didn't seem to be about solving puzzles, it was rather about optimizing a path.  I didn't have fun playing it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I remember when I first saw this game, we were doing the setup, explaining the rules and so on and my reaction was: my God, this is overwhelming, so many things to remember, so many little details! Indeed, this is a game that blends a whole bunch of mechanics at once which may seem a mess at first, but it really isn't. To my surprise, it became really smooth and fast-paced after just 3-4 turns! It reminds me of Scythe, although that game is less complicated in rules. The actions itself are fairly simple and you're basically doing one thing during the game - getting your way to Kansas. Of course, before you get there you do various things, such as acquiring cattle, building new stuff, trading with natives, selling cows that you don't actually need etc. The fun part is that you actually may choose your way - one time you travel slowly, doing a whole variety of actions, another time you skip everything and go to Kansas as fast as possible because you collected a nice hand of cattle(because there's an excellent deckbuilding element there) and you want to sell it and gain profits. Ending of the game is based on how often players visit Kansas so the result of that is players themselves determine the pace of the game. That's cool.

What I like is, while the game is not a sandbox - you really have to focus on one or two main things, there's still a significant sense of freedom in terms of what strategy you want to pursue. I played only twice, so I can't go much into details of various strategies, but from the first time I felt that there are at least a few viable routes and ways of getting points, which you can also efficiently combine. I can already tell that it's probably impossible to skip building your deck completely, because each time you visit Kansas you want to sell better set of cattle than before(otherwise you'll probably get penalties). However, that deckbuilding part can be accompanied by constructing buildings, moving forward your train(well, you need to deliver these precious cows) - that's probably the most fiddly part of the game which is unintuitive and takes a while to get into, getting rid of hazard tiles that represent threats like rockfalls - another source of points and it makes your way to Kansas easier, you can focus on objective cards... just too much to describe it here, not to mention that by doing many of these things you constantly evolve your personal player board, gaining access to stronger side-actions... I'm impressed how Alexander Pfister managed to combine all these things so they work together and all these interactions between them, yet the game remains straightforward if only things are explained carefully before the game. The drawback is the lack of help tiles that describe the iconography - you have to remember it. 

To sum up I even see GWT could become a 9 in my catalog, although I'd have to play it more. True, it's long, it becomes brainburning at the end of the game but weirdly enough it's not tiresome like Terraforming Mars and Nations that have the same issue. It's just pleasant to play and I can see why it's so high in the ranking. It's nice thematically, some things don't make sense though, particularly those connected with the trains and railways - but they, after all it's an euro game, it has to works mechanically and yes, it does very well. Great game.

RATING: 9
I disliked Mombasa from the same author as much as I really loved Great Western Trail. If Mombasa was a gratuitous mashup of popular mechanisms that didn't worked well together nor with the theme. Here, in sharp contrast, GWT has many mechanisms and subsystems that works very well together and moreover support the theme. The whole is better than the sum of its parts.

The turn sequence is really straightforward: 1/ move your character pawn, 2/ execute actions, 3/ refill your hand. The richness of the game comes from action 2 as depending on the space where you end your move you have a whole lot of actions to choose from. The net result is a smooth turn sequence with a lot of tactical and strategical choices.

The sheer number of options is a bit daunting for the first couple of play, afterward I really appreciated the diversity and refinement of the game. Early on you also need to learn the various pictograms used in the game, but I found them clear enough.

Overall GWT is both a game design achievement and a very enjoyable game to play and replay.

RATING: 8

I'm not sure what to rank Great Western Trail. I think it's a good game as a Euro/programming/puzzle with some variety, but it's not my favorite game in what's already my not-so-favorite genre of games. 

As with many of these games, I think it lacks the interaction for me and could of benefited from trading out one of its many point-salad paths to victory with a little more interaction (eg. pay to use other's buildings, or some blocking -- which is what I enjoy about Le Harve as a great Euro game.) Still, Great Western Trail gains points for a good theme, good iconography and pretty smooth gameplay once you get into it.  

Ultimately, though, if I'm going to play a game like this, I'd rather play Railroad Revolution, as it doesn't overstay its welcome, while offering a slightly cleaner but still challenging puzzle.

RATING: 9
Nothing particularly hard or counter intuitive in the rules, just lots of them, lots of bits. Actually playing the game is not too hard as you are always choosing from between a handful of decisions, all of which are usually a bit good, but not as good as the optimal move. This makes the game rewarding. The turns are also very quick. However the game is long anyway so additionally players adds mostly time rather than much else, though the game is not multiplayer solitaire. Theme is nice, nothing much more - it's mildly thematic. The components are good. The time does fly during playing this and it feels as if most strategies could work. I like getting cows and pruning, rather than building, I generally do poorly. Overall this is an excellent Euro game, preferable to terraforming mars in my mind, not that those two are really that comparable. Highly recommended.

Addendum, recent handful of plays at 2 player count, play time minus set up can go down to about 90 minutes, though I don't imagine it will go any lower; game is more strategic than I first thought, it's not heavy, its rich. Overal this is one of the best euros I have ever played, if you can get round the 'lot of simple' rules explanation. Great stuff.

RATING: 9
Mombasa was my favourite game from 2015. Could the on-form designer, Mr.Pfister, do it again? The answer is an unreserved ‘YES’. Great Western Trail is quite simply brilliant.

Great Western Trail is Mombasa-like in weight, possibly a little more complex, and Mombasa-like in the wonderful puzzle-type nature of the game. There is not a huge amount of interaction, less than Mombasa, but I rate this very highly indeed and look forward to exploring it some more over the forthcoming months.

The design is very clever: it is an interesting hybrid of action selection, card hand management, race, and player-board optimisation. There is, as you would expect, a ton of things to think about, and this is another game which could keep you up at night thinking through the options and what you might do different next time.

RATING: 4
2017-02-11 : I don't get the hype over this game. It's complex - lots of little mechanics and intricacies - but offers very little depth and is seriously hampered by the rather high luck factor. Draw the wrong cards at the wrong time and you're hosed. For instance : you need a good hand to be able to unlock the hand-size increasing spot which you'll need to build the even higher hand values. And that's just one example. This game is that damning combination of complexity and randomness I simply don't like. Compared to Mombasa - also complex - but where you can formulate a strategy and execute it, The difference cannot be more clearly illustrated as that in Mombasa a card you buy goes into your hand, guaranteeing you can play it at the most optimal time - in other words : long term planning - whereas in this game it goes into your discard pile and you may never see it again. So overall, this is clearly the lesser game and a shining example of a game that is in dire need of a developer who should have  streamlined it - a lot.

RATING: 6
SOLD IT!

The game has a bunch of working/scoring parts which is good. The ability to build the repeating board in a Caylus fashion is also a plus. While the idea of a player board that adds powers as it losses bits (Hansa Teutonica) is great in theory, the impact of these powers is very samey and not terribly exciting in Great Western Trail. Claiming cities upon cattle delivery late in the game is often frustrating... you can end up with one of your best made deliveries, only to be penalized by having to claim a lesser city since you already claimed the middle scoring ones in between. The achievement cards should always be a welcome reward, but in this game they can be a curse (think curse in Dominion) and often impossible to score ones acquired later in the game.

The game works and I get the hype, but it just feels a bit untested and flat in too many categories to be a "legend" as it sells itself as. Not sure it has earned the right to stay over other games in my collection,

RATING: 8
America in the 19th century: You are a rancher and repeatedly herd your cattle from Texas to Kansas City, where you send them off by train. This earns you money and victory points. Needless to say, each time you arrive in Kansas City, you want to have your most valuable cattle in tow. However, the "Great Western Trail" not only requires that you keep your herd in good shape, but also that you wisely use the various buildings along the trail. Also, it might be a good idea to hire capable staff: cowboys to improve your herd, craftsmen to build your very own buildings, or engineers for the important railroad line.

If you cleverly manage your herd and navigate the opportunities and pitfalls of Great Western Trail, you surely will gain the most victory points and win the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
After our first play, we thought the cowboy strategy was overpowered. Then I saw someone crush with a builder strategy. Then I crushed with a hybrid strategy. I can only assume that there’s an engineer strategy as well. In each of my four plays, people were standing up a lot while playing.

I like my deckbuilding when it’s built into the framework of a larger game, and that’s what you have here. You can add more powerful cards to your hand and cull the weaker ones. Cards are used for action spaces and for much needed income. 

At the same time, it’s not a requirement and you can go a different way and focus on hiring more workers. Cowboys make acquiring cards cheaper, craftsmen allow you to build better buildings, and engineers move your train (for different benefits). 

In addition to deckbuilding, there’s a nice spatial element in how you move around the board to different action spaces (that are randomized each game), making the board a rondel. The rondel aspect forces interesting timing questions for what to do with your money. You probably can’t buy new cards and hire workers this time around so which one do you do now? Players add buildings to the board that change things up and generally slow everyone down.
 
I’m excited to play again, and I can see room for an expansion of new action tiles.

Update: This is Best of 2016 for me. A keeper.

RATING: 8
1st play: :star::star::star::nostar::nostar:
3rd play: :star::star::star::star::nostar:
5th play: :star::star::star::star::star:
10th play: ???
14th play:  :star::star::star::nostar::nostar:

MAR 2017: After my 14th play, I am bumping the rating down to an 8, and wonder if it will drop to a 6 by next year. I still enjoy the challenge of the game, but am finding the variability of the game to be smaller than one would expect: only the cheaper buildings get built often in my play groups, which means we see the same ones over and over. The other sources of variability (cattle cards, bonus tiles, bonus cards, order of neutral buildings) offer minor "wrinkles" of variability.

DEC 2016 (later in the month): My intial impressions about the deckbuilding didn't do it justice. It's not a traditional deckbuilder - more of a deck management game, and it's growing on me quickly. What's best about this game is how many strategic approaches the game allows. I've seen high cattle win and low cattle win, buildings win and deliveries win. Really look forward to keep this game in the collection for a while.

DEC 2016: One solo play two-handed to get the hang of the game, and it didn't really grab me the way I was hoping it would. I was hoping for a broad and deep strategy game built around deck-building, but the deck-building isn't really and interesting driver of gameplay. They are just fuel for cash and some of the point-generation. Managing the other parts is more interesting. If those parts of the game (train, placement, upgrading buildings, movement pacing) prove to be tense and interesting, it might jump to an 8. 

OCT 2016: Visually, this game really sings! I'm fairly certain I'll like the mechanics in this game. The set-up and parts of the gameplay remind me [i]a lot[/i] of Terra Mystica, but more linear and slightly linear (since there is no map/expansion). My only real concern is that the series of small decisions that a player makes as they travel to Kansas City will feel too euro and/or not tight enough. If the tension is there, I will enjoy this game.

[b]I wrote a well-received preview, if any of you are interested:[/b]
https://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/1639154/preview-how-i-learned-lay-aside-my-dreams-cowboy-l

RATING: 6
Wow, there is a lot going on here.  This is a heavy, point salad, game, although i like it way better than any of Feld's theme-less games.  It's also long, about 3 1/2 hours for 4 players, 3 of whom were new.

Seems to be varying strategies that could all work.  I went with a building heavy strategy and wound up winning by about 6 pts.  No one  went heavy into cows or trains, so those options seem like other places to get big points.

I enjoyed the variety of the buildings, the limited building slots, and how you can chain buildings together.

Like Russian Railroads, this has the potential issue where if you try and equally do a little bit of everything (train, buildings, cows) you probably won't win over someone that was heavy into one of those and just a little into the others.

Definitely want to play again.  In terms of heavier Euro's, this one has a much more enjoyable theme.

On a second play, I lowered my rating a bit due to my poor planning.  I decided to go in to a heavy cow strategy but failed to realize the extra buildings we were using didn't have any cow buying spots on them or any worker buying spots.  I also erred in not plopping down some early buildings in the later half of the board which limited my money going into the worker buying location which for this play was right before Kansas City.

I still enjoy this game, although it is long.

RATING: 10
Another perfect 10 game for me. This is a great game for two players. It does run a little long, but even our first full game, after having a good grasp of the rules, lasted just under two hours. Not bad. I really like how you can mix and match different strategies throughout the game. I focused on a little of everything: a decent deck of cattle cards, three higher scoring buildings, and getting pretty far along the train track, with station upgrades. Can't wait to play again, and the game itself isn't that difficult at all once you get going. Up to the very last turn, I still found myself planning the right moves to maximize the amount of points I would finish with. Sure enough, I did win it on the last turn by upgrading an 8-pt. train station, which won it for me 106-102. A definite keeper! Highly recommend!

RATING: 7
Great Western Trail is an amalgam of several different mechanisms and pulls it all together in a nicely interlocking package. It allows for both specialization and a more varied approach but the one overarching requirement with everything you do is getting the timing right. Slow the game down in order to deliver a more valuable cattle herd to Kansas City? Accelerate in order to get first pick of the available helpers or contracts? Take a detour for a bonus action?

The somewhat distracting board makes grasping the game a little harder than it needs to be but the flow of the game is remarkably simple: advance pawn, take building action until you get to Kansas City, then restart from the beginning. And each time the landscape subtly changes.

RATING: 9
Spiel mir das Spiel vom Rind: Alexander Pfister macht sich mit einer Viehherde durch die Weiten und Weiden des Wilden Westen auf den Weg nach Kansas City, um dort weiter Siegpunkte für seine steile Karriere zu sammeln: mit GREAT WESTERN TRAIL tritt er was Anspruch und Spielintelligenz angeht in seinen Cowboyboots in die Fußstapfen seines Afrika-Smashers. Allein die Variable der Grundgebäude kreiert Gewichtungen und Abläufe. Wem der Schatten vom MOMBASA über GWT zu groß ist, dem  gibt allein das Thema Grund: "Um den Wilden Westen zu sehen, solange es ihn noch gibt“, wie der dreckige Indianderkumpel John Dunbar einst sagte. Great Pfister Game.

RATING: 6
Great Western Trail is a stunning achievement in design, as far as designing a game where a dizzying number of mechanisms are made to all work together. One of the larger point salad games I've played in recent memory that "feels" balanced.  It almost makes you wonder whether Pfister and the design team used a computer to help aid in the balancing, as there are numbers, actions, and opportunities for squeezing out points from everywhere and yet nothing felt overly powerful without dinging you somewhere else if you pursued only one road to points. I'm not a fan of paying off other players to move across their buildings, as money always felt tight and it was just that little extra meanness that made for a little extra pain. Still, it promotes players to race to that one objective (of many), so it is an obvious part of the game.  At times the mini-maxing can be grueling, but rarely more so than other games of this type. The overuse of iconography could easily be too much to handle for some, but it didn't wear as bad on me as I thought it might.  The fact that the publishers didn't include a symbol summary sheet is a little puzzling.  Overall, I'm impressed by the interworkings of this game and yet I'm not a real fan of how much the game relies on icon-heavy, chaining effects to get things done. Yes, this is a typical Euro thing, but with GWT it is almost too much.  The game is so overwrought on icon effects that it feels rather divorced from the theme, or at the very best it buries the theme.  It's just a hard game to get jazzed about as I need to feel the theme even a little, and GWT does nothing to make that happen.  Lastly, the game really does thrust the delivering of cattle as a main part of the game, but if one or two players pursue this relentlessly, the other players must switch up their long-term strategy if they want to stay in the competition. But the game doesn't really make this need explicit. It's something you have to learn over multiple plays, which is fine, but I do wish the game was a little more obvious with its paths to victory.  Pursuing a win in GWT can be opaque if you're forced out of going for the obvious "cattle" strategy.  Overall, GWT is a a game I'm impressed with in terms of mechanistic complexity, but the theme is obscured under the mechanisms so much so and the system is so overwrought at times that I'm not that motivated to experience it again any time soon.

RATING: 8.75
Prelim - Rating could easily rise.
Not a game dripping with theme or elegance, but one that presents some interesting choices once you start to understand the engine and scoring. Takes patience to learn, and even more to teach. From my limited plays, it seems to have struck a very delicate balance between different scoring opportunities, and the opportunities to gear your engine towards them. Player interaction is subtle but distinctly relevant. Managing the end game is difficult. Having made it through the rules and a couple plays now, I find myself getting sucked into this one, and don't see it letting up soon.

Follow-up:
This game reminds me in an odd way, of Power Grid. It's a clunky mix mechanics, but one that comes together very well. It does a great job with 'multiple paths to victory'. It's a bit to set up and tear down, and there's luck with both your personal card draws as well as tile draws, starting position, bonus cards, etc. Overall though, I can live with these for an interesting game experience. 
Note: we've house ruled that everyone gets the same number of turns, as first player would otherwise have a significant advantage in our view.

RATING: 4
Interesting mechanics, but the theme is totally botched. Most of the breeds of cattle in this game are DAIRY cattle! Not beef cattle! They needed to do a little bit of research to figure this out. They needed shorthorn and hereford. Not Jersey and Guernsey and Holstein and Ayrshire. No! Plus, cattle drives went to Dodge City and Abilene and Wichita, not Kansas City. KC was the first major stop on the railroad. KC had huge stockyards. From here, cattle were shipped to other locations, such as Chicago, for butchering. Again, they needed to do just a little bit of research. But they didn't do that. Also, the ranches that drove their cattle to the market towns had nothing to do with the railroads. And little to nothing to do with buildings erected along the cattle drive trails. But this was the era of the open range, when cattle roamed free. The game however is largely about cattle breeds that were were developed and managed in their own pens (or in other words, it's about the closed range, not the open range). This is a game for people who don't know and don't care about how cattle drives really worked. It's a game for people who care about game mechanics, and in that respect its mechanics are sufficiently complex to have attracted a lot of interest. But I struggle to care about how elegant the mechanics might be when I'm looking at a hand of dairy cattle for two plus hours. I wish the game was instead about getting the cattle to market in good shape so that they were healthy and fat. This would allow them to receive a top grade and thus top dollar. Rushing herds to market would be bad. They would be skinny and not command a good return. I can envision the game being about managing time so that you spend neither too long nor too short a period of time on the trail. While you have to deal with the threat of stampedes that might scatter cattle across the plains. You're trying to make sure the cattle eat and drink well throughout the trip, especially immediately before you drive the herd into Dodge City or Abilene or Wichita. You would also have to deal with rustlers. Where are rustlers in the game?

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.8
It's important to realize this is really a deck-building game in slow-motion. Some strategy hints:

Invest in enough temporary certificates early in the game to be able to reach your first city at 10 points that will allow you to increase your hand size. Once you do this, you need to increase the variety of cattle in your deck quickly, and invest in the double upgrade that allows you to cycle cards in your hand. This seems to be the most important early game strategy.

Synergistic placement of buildings is also key, as is placing buildings at the right point in your cycle so that you'll have the resources you need to take the action at that time. For example, you want to ensure that you have enough money when you visit the recruit or market actions.

Think about what you want to do right before getting to Kansas City and place buildings appropriately. Having a last chance to cycling cards in your hand if you need to seems to be a good choice.

Getting the right station agents can also pay off big at the end, so don't neglect them. Permanent certificates are very powerful, especially since removing the token on the level 5 certificate is an inefficient choice compared to other options. Don't focus on goal cards too early, as they will only clog up your deck and potentially give negative points if you play them and can't complete them.

Force yourself to move quickly to Kansas City--don't dawdle just because you can potentially do something along the way. Consider the tradeoff of getting to Kansas City and then back through the beginning of the trail (with recruit and build actions if you're playing the standard game) versus a few extra dollars or train movement points. Trading off velocity with actions is critically important; it can be tempting to take every action you can, but players who move faster will build their engines faster.

Don't be afraid to spend all your money--lack of money lets you pass other players' toll gates without helping them, and there are many opportunities to get more money along the way while also cycling cards. Through most of the game, if you have a lot of money sitting around, you're not being efficient enough.

Don't worry about the train too much; it will generally take care of itself as long as you stay close to the pack and can leapfrog opponents here and there. The upgrade that allows you to move the train forward a space is almost worthless (especially compared to the draw+discard upgrade) when you can move 2-3 spaces in one action by leapfrogging opponents.

Wasted turns if you plan poorly are an unfortunate problem, and players on a roll improve really quickly. Don't forget VPs on cattle, hazards, and buildings, but don't optimize for those until mid- to late-game.

RATING: 3
Many problems: Taxation is unreasonably punishing early in the game with players throwing down tax buildings in objectively-optimal spots. Cows of the same color have different point values for no reason other than to randomly reward players, and the same can be said of the hazards. Objectives can stall out with multiple San Francisco primary objectives clogging the queue, forcing players to take sub-optimal objectives. At the beginning of the game, hazards can be asymmetrically distributed, rendering some parts of the board inaccessible for building for 2/3 of the game. Cowboys are supremely expensive when compared to the net benefits that you accrue from builder (you overpay for cowboys so that you can 'overpay less' for cows). 

It's possible that there is a good game underneath all of this, perhaps for 2 players. I like the idea that players can set the pace of the game by cycling through a queue of possible actions. However, the game completely falters at 3 and 4 players simply because there are not enough optimal build spaces to go around; someone will always be shorted.

RATING: 8.5
A gamer's game, as it initially appears to be a heavy game, but after a few turns, everything becomes intuitive and makes sense.

Initial game rule explanation took about 30-40 minutes, but we covered everything.  Could be shorter as a lot of rules can be explained as we play the first few turns and make the first delivery.

Very good production value.
Rules are well written.
Excellent replayability (with random placement of neutral buildings)

Best to play with 2-3 players initially to learn the game, as 4 players may make the game a tad long, not to mention that it takes a game to get a good grasp of the value of the various placement locations, and how the game flows with regards to hazards, train movement/options, hiring crew, and the light deck building aspect.

All in all, a very good game experience.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
The game board is your rondel, with new pieces being constructed/added/removed over the course of the game or appearing as people make their laps around and around! After thinking about it like this, it was easier for me to process all the stuff going ons.

There is a side "deck building" aspect to it, but it is as minor as you want it to be. Everyone has the same slim deck of cards, which contain four different colors (cattle). When you get to the end of the board (Kansas City), you'll want to have as many different colors as possible in your 4-card hand because that's the gist of how to make more money/points. The game will be pushing you to make higher and higher deliveries each time. There is a space where you can buy more cards/cattle, and the ability to cull cards from your deck, but this is just one avenue to scoring your points. One of the players didn't cull any cards, acquired a few extra cattle, and focused more on building stations, and was 1 point behind first.

EDIT: After a few plays, it seems to be a good idea early on to focus on putting out buildings on the board and hiring builders--it will hurt a lot later in the game when the board gets clogged with other players' buildings. Taking the -6 pt delivery space isn't as detrimental as it sounds as it gives you $6 which can be extremely helpful early on (ie: essentially free worker).
I have tended to invest in the auxiliary actions that gives coins and in mid-game, trash cards; I rarely use the "draw one discard one" action, but it seems to be liked by a lot of people.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A kind of cross between Dominion and Perpetual Motion with a bit of Caylus thrown in. Not bad, but this game is unapologetically a snowball. There's no catchup, so it's essential to do well at the start, once you're behind you're contending for the minor placings. Increasing your hand size is particularly crucial, without it you'll never deliver the higher value herds. I've been saying for some time that the Eurogame design space is mined out, but as long as people keep buying, this is the kind of thing that will be produced.

RATING: 10
I was hesitant at first because of the cows / cowboy theme, which is not really my favorite theme. Yet the game completely convinced me - the first play was actually a bit bland and slow, but when you know the rules and when you randomize the neutral buildings it becomes so interesting! And even though you are mainly playing your own game, there is quite a bit of interaction for building spaces and train stations, trying to outthink the opponent and adjusting your plays.

The card drafting with the cows is also fascinating - exchanging cows for money during the trip, yet still trying to get a good hand for Kansas city. You can influence many things yet still depend on some of the randomness and need to adjust the strategy, which is engaging. Highly recommend!

RATING: 7
This game is fine. There's nothing wrong with it at all, but it's also just not very exiting. Standard euro stuff really, some deck building, some engine building, multiple paths to victory. And I love euros, but this was just nothing special. You can't really plan more than a few turns in advance, and there's no interesting mechanical twists anywhere.

There is some interaction in how and where you build your buildings. It's pretty basic but it's there.

Everything scores points, and mostly there are a bunch of ways to turn money into points once you're done spending money setting up your engine. But a big source of points is in making it to Kansas City, especially later in the game once you're making better deliveries. But the game has a variable end and what ends up happening is some players will be in the exact right place to end the game and others will be half way through. You get one last action after someone else ends the game but if you can't make it to Kansas City on that action then you're probably not getting more than a few points with it. And the weird thing is it feels like there's little planning you can do to make sure you're with the pack.

And in general the randomness was a bit high (in addition to your card draw there's also the cow offer, the person offer, and the goal card offer). Probably in tolerable ranges if the game were more interesting but it's not.

RATING: 8.6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a very thematic different sort of game.  You make maybe half a dozen trips along the trail to Kansas City to sell your herd of cow cards.  Along the way you visit a variety of buildings, either neutral and available to all or privately built and only for the owner, and perform a set of actions at the building.  When you sell your cows you get a lot of money, get vp, and unlock extra abilities on your board (like increasing cow hand size or more movement).  There are essentially three clear paths to victory, each requiring a different type of worker that you can hire: building buildings (craftsmen), buying better cows (ranchers), and moving your train to get to higher vp depots (engineers).  You can either dawdle at the buildings to use their actions or race through the journey to sell your cows more often.  This is a very strategy rich game with lots of options, especially with regard to the buildings you can place if you can set up a good combo.  I'm still wrapping my head around the ins and outs of this game but definitely can say I like it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Great Western Trail has a steep learning curve, partially due to an abundance of iconography and partially due to being somewhat unforgiving when making missteps. Work through those challenges and there's a game that provides a unique feel with a challenging tightrope to walk, forcing players to carefully time decisions on how much to build and how fast to travel based on how their opponents are playing. The game has a couple of blemishes (most notably how certain strategies such as buildings seem weaker than others), but it rewards frequent players with a varied, enjoyable challenge each game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
2017-06-18

Initial Rating: 7.0 (June 2017)

I was very keen to play Great Western Trail. It seems I enjoy a lot Alexander Pfister´s heavy games (his lighter games not so much although I still haven’t played Oh My Goods!). I really liked Mombasa and I really liked Great Western Trail.

The rules of the game aren’t very complex, but there are many rules and icons to explain. You can explain the game under 28 minutes. When a friend was explaining the game, I thought it could be a What’s Your Game? game. Playtime is around 3 hours, so I strongly prefer to play it with 3 fast players. I won’t ever again play it with 4 players. With 4 players, the downtime and playtime is just too painful.

The game´s components and art are ok. The board is gorgeous and looks great on the table.

The theme doesn’t do anything for me, but it is ok.

The game´s decision are very interesting and each one matters a lot and has deep reaching consequences. In your turn, you must advance along the path and then perhaps do an action. At the beginning, moving is quick an easy, but later in the game there are a lot more buildings and obstacles and you have to pay money many times so planning how to move is key. Among the available actions you have: use a neutral building, your building or the auxiliary actions. You can get better cards, hire people (you have three options), build buildings, advance your train, build an station, get money, get better abilities on your player board or continue to advance during the trail. Many actions require you to play cards. Once you get to Kansas City you deliver your cows and you will be able to reach a city to get money and VPs. Doing this efficiently and quickly is not an easy task and solving this puzzle is very fun and rewarding. You have many paths to take and the best one isn’t obvious. I really like it.

There is some luck with the game (workers, hazards, teepees, draw of the cards), but I feel the best player will always win, that is, the luck factor can be controlled and the player that plans better will win.

Great Western Trail has a high replayability due to the buildings, they have 2 sides and they can be chosen at random as long as all players play with the same ones. There is also a random setup, so each game will be different.
 
It is a bit weird that the player´s boards are two player by default, it is usually the other way around. If you are playing a three or four player game you need to use additional tiles. Perhaps, it is Pfister´s way to tell us that GWT is best as a two player game, perhaps it is :D.

Bottom line, I really like Great Western Trail. It has very interesting options and many paths to victory. It is, definitively, a keeper among my game collection and a great strategy game. I really want to play it soon again.

Current Rating: 7.5

[b]Jgoyes´ Awards: [/b]

[imageid=3812560inline]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
My pick for best game of the Essen 2016 season.

Great Western Trail pulls some mechanics from Caylus (the variable building path that is partially player-constructed) and deckbuilders (the herd deck) but has a flavor all its own, with micro-turns, action chaining, and gameplay that is strategic as well as intricately tactical.  Once the players are familiar enough to randomize setup the game will literally never be the same for the first hundred plays or so, as there are 1024 different private building combinations in addition to the 28 different permutations for the general buildings.

Considerations include building such as to provide oneself meaningful action chains while placing stumbling blocks in the way of opponents, strategic decisionmaking in which type of worker is the focus (as well as a sub-branch for the engineers of focusing on station tiles or long train distance), and of course the usual deckbuilding, which is highly streamlined in itself but interlocks nicely with the rest of the system.

Great Western Trail is more generally accessible than the designer's other well-regarded game Mombasa; there is no problem of similar upfront complexity to Mombasa's action selection and bookkeeper tracks.  This should keep the playtime under 2.5 hours once the players are skilled, while Mombasa for all its greatness still struggles to get under 3 hours.

Questions have arisen in my local group whether bad hand draws can put a player irrevocably behind.  I don't know for myself whether I find this truly concerning yet - I've had good luck and mediocre luck but not the truly horrendous hands my opponents have experienced, so I don't have much firsthand perspective on playing around bad draws.  Until I've played out that side of a game, I'm not sure how I'll feel about the hand-draw randomness.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
GWT offers a circular, rondel-esque action structure that players build with action tiles as the game progresses. While the trail of actions is one way, it always ends in a delivery of cattle and then starts over again. Players cycle through about 5-7 times before the end conditions are met.

The structure of the game is simple and once you get going the game plays fast. However, there are a lot of options and a lot of symbols to learn, so the game may appear a little daunting at first. While there are many ways to get points, there are few key sources where the majority of points will come and how you choose to focus on these areas is the key to doing well. Players are managing and improving a deck of cattle cards that represent their herd. Building your herd and managing your hand to optimize cattle deliveries is one source. Another is advancing your train to better and better stations. Putting out more of your buildings (action spaces) is another possible source. And another source is gaining and playing goal cards. There are a number of other secondary ways to collect points. If you balance your focus between all of the main sources, you are unlikely to do well. As with a lot of these games, the key is optimizing your decisions to primarily support one or two of these sources while keeping an eye on your opponents and hindering them when you can.

While at first glance a mechanical mish-mash, Pfister has done a very good job of melding the various elements into a cohesive whole.

(Reminder to pick up fixed tile when Stronghold makes it available...)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Easily the best of the 2016 releases that I have played thus far -- rating based on 8 plays in less than two weeks after first sight (first four plays with a borrowed copy at EuroQuest convention).  I really like this Alexander Pfister design which captures the theme of the Old West with some really smooth and elegant game mechanics that make an appealing game.  First, there's the worker market that also works as a game-timing device -- brilliant design featue.  Then, there is the cattle market where you can add better cards to your deck based on number of cowboys you recruited.  Also, there is the unlocking of special abilities on your board each time you deliver to Kansas City and/or move your train to a special location.  The placement of buildings on the trail heralds back to that early worker placement game, Caylus; and then there's the deck-building aspect of Dominion added to a very intriguing board game.  Some have also made the analogy to some of Mac Gerdts' rondel games, which is mimicked by the important player decision as to how quickly to proceed during each "lap" down the road to Kansas City.  As a result, this game has quickly moved to the top of my list of best new games for 2016.  There's almost endless replayability due to a variable combination of available player buildings and random neutral building layouts.

I don't give out 9s lightly, but this game deserves such a high rating.  I enjoy the theme and interlocking mechanics in this one better than the award-winning Mombasa by the same designer.  Definitely better than games like Scythe and Terraforming Mars, which seem to be getting more love on BGG right now.  Hopefully, that will change as more people get to try this challenging game.

OK, second time re-rating this game, upping to the extalted status of a "10" -- it's clearly the best new boardgame since Puerto Rico in 2002, at least in my book.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
There are lots of options and things to learn. The interplay between the different strategies is interesting and how many people are pursing each changes the dynamic quite a bit.

There is player interaction, mostly in the form of "I bought this so you can't". Building buildings on the board is the other main source of player interaction, which is more nuanced than it may seem at first. Your buildings might cause opponents to pay a toll to move through, but they also slow them down. Different strategies need to visit Kansas city more frequently so buildings also offer a way to manipulate the effectiveness of the various strategies.

The game has a lot of little rules but they all hang together well and a single turn is a very simple affair. Move, activate building, draw. The devil is in the details of the actions on buildings. Turns are also surprisingly fast leading to a very smooth game flow. Things slow down a bit when someone reaches Kansas city, but even that only takes about 20 seconds.

Lots of people at the con had concerns about play length. My games were not overly long. The first was 2.5 hours and the second was 2 hours, both with 4 players. The first had all new players and the second had 3 new players. So I didn't experience the game dragging at all, but that is a common complaint so if you have AP issues in your group perhaps this one will drag. 

I'm very interested in playing this more. There's lots to learn about how to play well and I like that. I have yet to see someone build the really expensive buildings. The game allows you to focus on one area or to diversify and I think all sorts of approaches are valid, the question is: which will work best given the game state and what your opponents are doing?

RATING: 8
This and [gameid=177736] are the two heavy euro gaming behemoths of Spiel 2016. Be prepared to spend 1 hour per player on its first outing. Even after one play I'm reluctant to replay it if some of the players need a rules explanation. When I reentered the hobby almost ten years ago, heavy euros like Amun-Re or Brass needed far less explanations. Vinhos et al. have set a disturbing trend. Not that Great Western Trail isn't worth it. In the opposite; it's a good, potentially very good game. I just wish that it had a bit less overhead.

At its heart is a board that essentially is a huge developing rondel from which players choose their action. I initially had the impression that the development of the board was inspired by Caylus but William doesn't think so. Another essential system is the deck-building and the ensuing hand management. Furthermore, players have to improve their personal boards Hansa Teutonica-style. Three principle strategies are available to aim for: cattle, locomotive, building (in ascending sophistication/difficulty). The good news is that mixed strategies seem to be feasible as well as long as the player manages to squeeze out benefits a bit more efficiently from the game systems.

I only played its predecessor Mombasa once. My impression is that Great Western Trail is a more focused design while Mombasa had more player interaction.

2017-10-21 - 7->8 - I played again and I really enjoyed it! For its weight the turns can run really quick. It's a really good eurogame with low interaction, which is not my favourite genre, but from time to time I like to play such a a game and GWT is one to consider in such a case.
