RATING: 8
Alexander Pfister games have been a big hit for me over the last couple years, Broom Service and Mombasa rank among my favourites and Port Royal is a favourite filler. So I definitely had high expectations for Great Western Trail, even though I lost horribly on my first play the game hit the right notes with the strategic planning and brisk pacing. It's a game that really rewards dabbling in all the scoring options while focusing on one main route to score points, the scoring is very diverse so you can't ignore aspects of the game (as I found out in my first game). The teaching is a bit involved and can take about 20–25min to introduce players, but after a few rounds the turns speed up. But even though the turns are pretty quick, the game is pretty long, with three players it's about the perfect length for a medium-heavy Euro, with 4 it becomes longer than Mombasa. In terms of gameplay, there are lots of moving parts in this game and juggling trying to make the most of each action spot as well as trying to get the perfect hand of cows to deliver by the end of the 'rondel' of the trail is great. The drawing of cows at the end of your turn really is the best part of the game though!

[size=12][b]Top 100-200 board games list[/b][/size]

[size=12][b]Why I like Great Western Trail:[/b][/size]
+ The various mechanics (some of which I dislike) worked better than I anticipated. 
+ I could handle the complexity of the game
+ There was decent player involvement
+ The game requires meaningful decisions to win
+ I am making an exception for the duration because the game was interesting enough. 

[size=12][b]Fixable dislikes in regards to Great Western Trail:[/b][/size]
/ To improve the replayability, I am interested in a different map (that is more exciting) and a range of building tiles. There is apparently an expansion in the works that might address this dislike. If this happens I plan to improve my rating to an '8'.

[size=12][b]Why I dislike Great Western Trail:[/b][/size]
- The game is hard to teach (or at least takes a long time to teach) even with a good teacher.

RATING: 8
Initially seems very daunting with many different things to do but once you've got your head around the rules the game flows quickly. Whilst you will have a general game strategy, each turn you can only do a limited amount as you can only move a limited number of spaces, do actions on the space you stop at and re-fill your hand. As your moves will be relatively unaffected by other players you can plan your move whilst you wait, minimising the time it takes to do your go. Biggest negative is there's no catch-up mechanic if someone is a run away leader.

RATING: 7
This one is quite over-hyped and over-rated.  Not a bad game, but my least favorite from this designer.  Mechanics are interested and the turn marker track with the people to hire is extremely clever.  Sadly, the game essentially plays the same every time and gets old after just a couple plays.  The deck building mechanics is necessary but uninteresting (a cow is a cow is a cow) and interaction between players consists of mildly competing for persons/cows/stations and blocking each other with buildings, which simply isn't that interesting.  I'm sure fans will disagree but balance doesn't seem spectacular, with cowboys being a must and builders being the weakest path.  A bit like Orleans, this game gives the illusion of multiple paths to victory but that all really fall apart once you play with a strong player who really knows what to do to optimize his score.

RATING: 8
Ein Spiel das mir sehr gefällt! Deckbuilding und Aktionsmanagement.
Jeder Spieler erhält ein gleiches Deck an Karten von denen er am Zugende auf sein Handlimit aufzieht, wenn er denn Karten ausgespielt hatte.
Im Spiel läuft man den Trail entlang um auf Häusern zu landen und dort Aktionen auszuführen, wie z.B.: neue Häuser bauen, neue Karten fürs Deck aus dem Viehmarkt kaufen, Handkarten ausspielen um Geld zu erhalten, und und und.
Am Ende des Trails ist das Ziel, so viele unterschiedliche und hochwertige Karten auf der Hand zu haben, wie möglich. Jede doppelte Karte zählt 0. Dafür erhält man Geld und Siegpunkte. Wer am Ende am meissten von letzterem hat gewinnt.

Das Spiel geht etwas lange. Wenn man weiss was man tut dauert es wesentlich kürzer, zieht sich aber gegen Ende trotzdem, weil der Trail sich durch Bau von Häusern verlängert und man zwanglläufig länger braucht um ans Ziel zu gelangen.
Mir gefällt das Spiel dennoch sehr gut. Es gibt etliche Möglichkeiten seine Ziele zu erreichen. Man kann auf Bahnhofsbau gehen, wofür man Geld und Zugstrecken braucht, oder man dünnt sein Deck aus um schnell an Geld zu kommen, verschifft dann aber sehr Effektiv seine Rinder zu hochwertigen Städten, es gibt Auftragskarten die man erfüllen kann, Häuser bauen und sich eine gute Engine überlegen, und und und...
Mir hats gefallen, auch wenn es etwas kürzer sein könnte (dann hätt ich aber mein Deck nicht komplett ausdünnen können... gewonnen habe ich trotzdem nicht, sondern Mr. Bahnhofsstrategie...)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The game is exciting and varied, and also plays well with two. The players drive their flock from Texas to Kansas City, where the cattle are then delivered to distant cities by train. Each city can only be delivered once, so you have to plan well and you have to move your 'railhead' forward in time to avoid extra costs. The value of your hand with cows cards must also match the value of the city you deliver, otherwise your cows will go to lesser destinations with some penalty points. You increase the value of your hand by adding new, expensive cows to your herd and paying off in good time. During the trip from Texas to Kansas City you can visit general buildings to perform actions such as selling cows, recruiting cowboys, workers and engineers, building your own buildings at locations along the 'Trail', buying new, expensive cows that deliver a lot and increase your reach on the track. When you deliver a city or station, you place a sheet of your tableau at that location. As a result, you unlock an 'action improver' that allows you, for example, to hold more maps or to travel on your journey to the Kansas City delivery point, where the herds depart by train to their final destination. During the game the players make 5 to 7 trips to Kansas, allowing them to visit their buildings several times to use the advantage there. Great Western Trail is exciting and offers many tactical possibilities. The own buildings are two-sided: an easy and a difficult side. Although it seems complex, the game easily plays away and continues to fascinate time after time. However, there are better western games I think

RATING: 4
In Great Western Trail, you are a cattle rancher, driving your stock from Texas to Kansas City. You have to get them through hazards, do some canny cattle trading along the way, hire hands to manage the stock, and hope for a big payday once you get to the end of the trail!

And you build buildings. You make buildings or towns or something along the way, because that's what cattle ranchers did, they built towns and such and hired workers to help build them. I guess? Look, just focus on the cattle drive for the moment. We need to get to Kansas City!

And build a railway. Because the goal of a cattle drive was to go to a city that didn't have a railway and construct one. And along the way you'd traditionally build stations, which you could then man with your train engineers, builders, or cowboys. Cowboys were all the time leaving the profession in order to become station agents. Look it up.

In addition to these tasks that your basic ranchers had to perform, they would also create and then clean up hazards, trade with natives, and manage an employment office. The life of the average rancher was full of diverse responsibilities.

What I'm trying to say here is that GWT is a thoroughly modern board game trading on the latest trend: more is more. Why merely put the players in a single, well-defined, approachable role when instead they can play as, I don't know, "The Spirit of the Western Frontier" or something. Why give a clear goal -- you're looking to make your fortune driving beeves to market -- when you can instead provide ELEVEN different scoring categories to sum up? Why give a bonus for trading with natives when you can have two different kinds of natives for no reason other than more natives is better? Why make a game that can be taught and learned easily when you can instead add unnecessary wheels and levers and gears in the name of "depth"? 

There's a good and interesting game hidden inside of GWT, but it's buried under a lot of -- well, there's a thematic element I could say it's buried under, but why commit to a theme now? Instead you're given a myriad of options and paths, but no method for really exploring any of them. If you're trying to do the thing the game is ostensibly about -- sell cows -- you're going to want a lot more hand/deck management than you're allowed. If you want to build buildings, you have to make a lot of money, which means you have to sell cows as well. If you want to run a railroad, that's great, but you're going to have to do other things to score some points. And of course the objective cards want you to do a little of everything because focus? We don't need no stinkin' focus.

I'd like to really like GWT, but like so many current board games, it collapses under its own weight. So much incoherent nonsense has been added in that playing the game is less fun than frustrating, mostly because you're not competing against the other players but against the design of the game. Too many times it wasn't other players that were my issue (at least not by their intention) but because the game was asking me to do something convoluted that required the precise alignment of stars to get right. 

An editor with a red pen could have made this into a really great game. Instead we got yet another flavor of the week puzzle box that will be long forgotten when the next such game comes out. It'll get bought, learned, played a couple times, then hang out on a shelf for the rest of its life, constantly getting passed over because, "I don't even remember how to play that."
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Initial impressions: Wow what a well designed games. Like most modern euros it is a hodge podge of mechanics, but here outside of some of the set collection the hodge podge makes thematic sense. The deckbuilding ties into the managing your herd, the hand management that different towns want different types of cows, the time track that you must drive your cattle to new pastures to feed, the different action buildings that are you are making business contacts with settlements along your journey, etc. The idea of the rail track is thematic and that some cities will only accept higher value cows, but that you can only deliver to each city once not so much. Similarly it makes sense you would be rewarded for removing hazards on a route, but that you just pay money to do so is clearly just for the sake of mechanisms. 

It seems like you will be pretty lost your first few games as the different mechanisms interact in strange ways. I learned I was moving too quickly through the time track without delivering enough quality cows, which cost me greatly. The game also punishes mistakes somewhat harshly. However, it seems like there are quite a few different point scoring options you can pursue, as while you still have to do a little of everything, you can still get a different bulk of your points from different methods. I'm a little afraid if I ever get to that level of expertise the game may still feel a bit too arbitrary since the scoring is arguably the least thematic aspect of the game, but that probably is along ways off into the future, as different starting map configurations I would assume really change up the game.

A light deck building game where you try to move cattle from Texas to Kansas City and then to points west.  You can move on a board gathering cattle cards and building businesses along the trail.  You also move your train further west to get discounts on delivery costs or bonuses.  

The game is incredibly fun. I have found that I really like Alexander Pfister's heavier euros.  Isle of Sky was not that interesting but Great Western Trail is the best game of 2016.  

It plays in a reasonable amount of time and has a lot of interesting decisions.  Excellent graphic design, solid artwork and great theme.  This may crack my top ten after more plays.

And so I get distracted and do other things.  (Yes, I'm not very disciplined.)

RATING: 8
Great game - Each player has a there own deck made up of cows with a colour and a value - Follow the trail from building to building using the ability on the building to either build your own buildings or hire staff (cowboy - helps you buying new cows, builder - helps with buildings and engineer - helps move your train)- when you reach the end of the trail add up the value of each unique cow in your hand - that value is how far you can travel down the train tack to sell your cattle. Its also how much you earn minus certain points on the track from where your own train is. then move you guy back to the beginning of the trail and do it all again - Each player has his own board each time you either sell cattle of move to a station you can remove disks from your board which will allow you to unlock certain abilities - like increase hand size or increase movement etc etc 

great game with many ways to score

RATING: 7
Tentative rating after 2 plays (7.5): Very straightforward to play (once you get a grasp of the icons).  Turns are quick.  I tend to be a little bit picky when it comes to medium to heavy euros games.  I really only want a handful of great ones in my collection so I can play them over and over.  Still to be determined if this can offer the same amount of enjoyment as Concordia, Puerto Rico, or Hansa Teutonica.  Plan to play this several times more before making any sort of real decisions about its longevity.  I enjoyed it more than Mombasa.

Update after 3 plays (7): The 3rd play didn't excite me so I decided I was done with it.  A lot of newer medium to medium-heavy euros seem to be flopping with me.  Tired of games that try to throw too many mechanisms together.  Never feels truly compelling too play.  They lose a lot of luster & depth once you 'figure' out how all the different mechanisms work together.  

I love Port Royal and Broom Service, but Isle of Skye, Mombasa, and Great Western Trail just didn't offer enough enjoyment to keep in my collection.

RATING: 8
I usually don't like games with this much of a sandbox feel, but this one has me both mesmorized and frustrated, in a good way. I really don't know whether to go heavy cattle, heavy train, heavy building, or a little bit of everything. I don't know whether to rush to KC or take my time. I basically have no clue what I'm doing, but keep finding myself eager to play again to learn a new trick or two.

--------------------

Some stuff we did wrong in our first few games:

You only get a single auxiliary action at someone else's building or at a hazard/teepee, not a double.

You don't draw back to your hand limit until Phase C (could impact options for buildings with movement).

Multiple people can have a disk at a single train station. Also, you don't have to be station master and take the tile while giving up a worker. You might just want to clear off a disk to gain a perk on your personal board.

The permanent purple shares you can earn from station master tiles are separate from the temporary ones marked with the cube on your player board.

The woods private building tile pays $2 for each of your private tiles in the woods, not just tiles with that icon.White disks can go anywhere, black disks go on black spaces only.You may discard objective cards via the draw/discard action (available as auxiliary and on some building tiles).

RATING: 9.5
I'm really loving this game. This game is a bit of a beast to teach, but I've thoroughly enjoyed each play and love that every single turn provides me with interesting choices and tense decisions.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This is an absolutely fantastic and engaging game. You are moving your cows on a trail to Kansas city, stopping along the way to manipulate your herd (hand of cattle cards), hire helpers, building buildings, buying more prestigious cows, trading with natives, clearing hazards, expanding your rail line and so forth. Whenever you get to the end of the trail you sell and ship off your cows, which earns you money of course, but also unlocks/upgrade abilities on your player board.

This is a game that takes a few hours to play usually, but I'm utterly engaged the whole way and it doesn't feel like that much time has passed. I'm engaged in every single aspect of the game, there are so many different paths you can take, different things you can focus on, and I just love it. One of my favourites.

RATING: 8
Excellent execution of a rondel system in a board game. I love how you can build up you options as you move around the board as the game matures. Like many of my favorite games there are many paths to victory and i have indeed found my favorite strategy. I am definitely trying to get this to the table but my reason why i don't is also one of my negatives about this game. This game is a bit rough to teach. So all in all I love this game but I always do not want to teach it. If i don't have to teach its definitely getting to the table.

RATING: 8.75
Got in a few more plays (with fewer than 4 players) and am really getting into this game. If offers a million ways to win but doesn't feel like point salad. The various worker types (Engineer, Craftsman, Cowboy) are all distinct and interesting, almost like three games wrapped up into one. Long-term strategy is really important but there's also a lot of flexibility, allowing you to shift away from a strategy that isn't working out. Especially near the beginning of the game, there's the constant question of, "Do I have to stop here, or should I skip it to get to Kansas City faster?" (For income and upgrades). There's also something satisfying in getting into a rhythm, with certain locations that are very important for your strategy. But you still have to adapt and evolve that strategy as new buildings are placed as well as consider what's in your hand. Fairly simple rules but a lot of complex interactions.

RATING: 7
Finally an Alexander Pfister game I like! That isn't to say that this is one of my favorite Euros ever or anything, but I do think it's his best design yet.

There's some Euro junk rattling around, however. The game feels a bit like a kitchen sink design, and I think that some parts of it could have been cut out, despite all feeling connected to the theme. I think the game is pretty long for what it is, and after awhile the progression of the game slows to a crawl.

That all being said, I really like the way the Rondel is utilized here. Being able to build locations along the route to Kansas City, and choosing whether or not they are tucked behind obstacles is very interesting. The deck building element, while fairly minor, feels pretty fresh. I also like the various strategies to pursue, but again, the game is so long that it doesn't feel like you're agonizing over which actions to take. You have enough time to where you can pursue pretty much everything to some degree.

I do think that a second edition of this game with some further development could really make this into a classic. As it is, this is a fine Euro game that is surprisingly thematic. I wouldn't be opposed to playing again, but the length is a pretty big turn-off for me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
At first glance, Great Western Trail offers many interesting mechanics that blend very well. You have an action rondel that you can move through at your own pace. You have abilities to unlock and buildings to build to improve your actions. You have a hand of cards that you can optimize during the game to improve your sales. Some rules are a bit fiddly and convoluted but once you understand the flow, the turns are smooth and quick. All in all, you'll never find yourself out of interesting decisions during a game of Great Western Trail.

But in spite of being a game about getting your herds to the markets before your opponents, there is very little interaction in Great Western Trail. Everybody can sell in a city, everybody can build a station and everybody can use neutral buildings (but nobody else but you can use your own buildings). Why no fluctuating market prices? Why no shared infrastructure? Add to this the all too common point salad where you score for everything and you have a solitaire optimization game.

Great Western Trail is a solid game and I'd be happy to play it if asked but there is nothing that really makes it stand out.

RATING: 10

RATING: 9.5
After half a dozen or so plays, I am surprised that each game has revealed new strategies. The whole game is a series of relatively simple decisions, while having to balance the medium-term challenge of racing to Kansas or stopping everywhere, and the long-term of doing discs and collecting VP's. Very clever game design.

Regarding game play itself, there is generally little blocking, so you are usually ready with a move when it is your turn. Decisions increase smoothly as the game progress, and the end game is full of excitement, as the scores are obscured, and there will be cries of 'I didn't realise I was doing so well', or 'if only I had one more dollar at the end'. You can sometimes feel things aren't going your way, but this is sometimes a false assumption, and I've not seen players feel they can't catch up.

Some comments on what I think may discourage someone from trying the game. I have heard a lot of people complain that the theme is of no interest to them. This is by no means a thematic game, but it does tie all the elements of the game together, allows a few jokes/puns, and the game play is totally worth trying. Also, regarding the iconography, it certainly isn't familiar too many people. But I've found that after teaching the game and explaining the icons, everyone has 'clicked' with the icons, and no one has reported difficulty with legibility. One unusual aspect is how the money cost/reward is noted, but pointing this out early helps and all players have quickly adapted.

RATING: 5
first impression, lukewarm. it had a solitaire feel. strange mixture of a game. while managing your deck of cards, you are going round and round a board, visiting buildings that enable you to build more buildings, pay for and acquire more cards, acquire helpers you put on your player board, advance your engine on a track, that allows you to develop your player board and also score endgame points, acquire tiles that score endgame points and acquire money. the elements are nicely meshed together, and provide stuff to think about and replayability. There is some competition for stations on the engine track, for better places to build on the board, for getting cheaper helpers and for higher value tiles, but there is always something to do and your actions seem incremental, I never felt, that if my opponents take something away I could lose, so did not need to keep a close eye on what they were doing. I do not like the deck building element and the somewhat repetitive nature of the game.

RATING: 6
[b]Great Western Trail[/b] 

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (6):[/COLOR]

[i]I don't really have much to say about Great Western Trail; I was excited to play and ended up being completely underwhelmed by the experience. In the end I found it to be a very mediocre experience that really didn't add anything to the game space it occupies [i](mid-weight Euros)[/i]. 

I found GWT to be a hodge podge of mechanics that felt completely disjointed without any cohesiveness. With some games that doesn't bother me, but I didn't enjoy the progression in GWT. The actions felt very similar from the beginning of the game to the end, with very little progression or engine building. I found the deck building aspect to be very disappointing; all I did was continually cycle through my deck on the way to KC in order to have a variety of different cows in my hand. On top of that the game never fought back, you could pretty much figure out a way to do what you wanted. I much prefer games that are nastier, for example I enjoy figuring out how to feed my family in Agricola.

I have little interest in playing GWT again or even trying Mombasa, but I really enjoy Pfister's lighter, family weight games [i](Broom Service, Isle of Skye and I'm excited to play Oh My Goods)[/i] so I'll just stick with those.[/i]

RATING: 8
Spiel '16
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Un interesante euro de complejidad media-alta, con una combinación de mecánicas que da para todos los gustos. Podés trabajar tu mazo de ganado para aumentar su valor y abrir nuevos mercados, podés trabajar tu ferrocarril para llegar más lejos y más barato, podés construir edificios a lo largo de la ruta a Kansas para tener más acciones y cobrar peajes, podés comerciar con los indios o sacarlos a balazos de tu camino (aunque en ese caso los llamamos "peligros", a caballo, con pluma en la cabeza, pero no indios), podés mejorar tus habilidades en base a la experiencia de cada vuelta del viaje que hagas, o podés hacer todo eso a mismo tiempo, combinando un efecto con otro, si querés tener una chance de ganar.

Los turnos muy son cortos pero las opciones se complejizan rápidamente, y hay un elemento táctico en un tablero compartido que cambia constantemente o te da un pesito menos o un pesito más, que puede dar para algo de análisis parálisis.

Me hizo algo de ruido la gran diferencia en el funcionamiento de la engine del juego, entre el setup predefinido y el setup aleatorio. El predefinido fluye como una seda, armás combos y vez lucirse la magia. El aleatorio puede generar escenarios frustrantes de engines empastadas donde nada de esa maquinaria se luce. No estoy seguro cómo se podría asegurar un término medio para no comerse una partida que deje una mala impresión del potencial del juego para desenvolverse y enriquecer la experiencia. Jugar el setup predefinido solo es algo limitado. Tampoco escala bien. O mejor dicho, no escala.

En resumen, muy buena temática y producción, la engine no es un reloj suizo pero camina. Recomendable. No se si categoría "juego del año".
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
規則:
*隨機放置五個車站大師板塊在圖板上方格，七個中立建築板塊隨機放置在暗色的圖板建築格(第一次建議照字母放置)，工作標記放在左邊工作軌最上方格，工人/帳篷/災難板塊蓋著照後面數字分類洗勻，先開數字1的，帳篷放印地安軌從-3開始放，災難則放到對應軌從數字小的開始放(滿了蓋著放回去)，直到開七張，再開2號，放到工作軌對應人數行最上面格，往左補在往下補，直到有工作標記那行滿，之後在從1/2/3分別開兩個放到圖板左上預見區對應格，36個市場牛卡洗勻放圖板下方，2/3/4人時開7/10/13張，然後分類左到右顏色為，黃紅藍棕紫，24目標卡洗勻放圖板右邊開四張
*每個玩家選一個顏色拿圖版跟配件，3人以上拿一個玩家計數板塊用對應人數面，每人拿10個自己顏色的私人建築板塊(第一次建議使用A面)，之後可以任意組合但所有玩家要一樣，顏色圓片放在個人圖板對應顏色格，火車放主圖版起始火車格，認證方塊放到個人圖板認證軌最上方格，米寶放一旁，每人再拿14張起始牛卡洗勻抽四張，洗勻四張起始目標卡隨機發一張開著，隨機起始拿七元，照順位每人遞增一圓
*遊戲照順位進行，每個玩家回合有三個階段
A 沿著鐵路移動米寶   每個經過地點算一步，至少一步，上限在個人圖板上，只能在線上移動，移動到堪薩市時就要停留
*第一次進場放到任意中立建築板塊
*經過或停留的地點如果上面有手，則根據玩家人數要支付金額，是別人的地點錢就付給他，如果沒錢就不用付
B 使用停留地點的功能
a中立或你的建築  執行上面的所有行動，要支付的要能付才能做，也可不做，或是全部不做改執行附屬行動，在個人圖板上左邊
*如果建築地點下方有額外行動也可選擇做
b 別人建築物或災難板塊 執行附屬行動
c 堪薩市 先把預見區每個數字的板塊選一個放到對應位置(金額或數字最小格，工人優先放工作標記那行對應人數位置，左到右放，如果要放到工作標記則標記下移一格)，之後開手牌拿收入，然後手牌棄掉，然後可以放一個圓片到主圖板上方城市格，之後米寶放回主圖版右下牛仔格，並補充預見區
*當工作標記超過黃色箭頭時根據人數補牛卡區，牛卡牌庫沒牌就不補，如果一超過紅色箭頭則觸發遊戲結束
*拿收入時每種顏色的牛卡算一張，總值相加，可支付任鎮軌點數加值，車站大師板塊則永久加，根據此數值拿錢
*根據你收入使用的牛值，選擇一個城市值小於或等於此值且你沒放過圓片的(第一個跟最後一個可以重複放)，放圓片到該格上(白色背景可到任意格，黑色背景圓片只能到黑底數字城市)，且馬上啟動該格獎勵(相鄰箭頭獎勵要剛好兩格都有時啟動)，並且你放的位置到你火車頭所在位置中有幾個叉要付多少錢(火車頭上的不算)
**行動列表  紅色代表支付，白色或綠色代表獲得，有顏色代表要出對應顏色
>棄一張黑牛卡拿兩元
>棄兩張同色牌拿四元
>棄一張任意牌認證軌加一
>棄一張灰色卡，認證加1跟拿兩元
>棄一張灰色卡，火車頭前進一格
>雇用工人  拿工作標記該行以外的任意工人，支付右邊價格，放到個人圖板對應工人行最左邊格，如果放置格有獎勵行動馬上執行
>蓋建築  選一個個人建築蓋到場上空格或是你的建築上，工人要符合左上需求數目，每個要付兩元，如果蓋在之前的上面則只要付差額跟工人差數，被蓋掉的移除
>拿目標卡 選一張開著的或抽頂牌放到個人棄牌堆，並馬上補到四張
>移動火車頭 移動數目等於你有的工程師數目，移動格數會跳過有人的位置，移動時可以移到車站位置(一樣要花步數)，可少移動，如果停在車站且上面沒有圓片可升級之，支付上面金額放一個圓片上去(白色黑色規則同城市)，如果上面有車站大師板塊可放一個你個工人版塊取代之
*火車軌最後一格到時一定要停住，獲得三元，然後後退至少一格，可停到任意格，如果停在車站也可升級
>印地安交易 移除一個帳篷板塊放到你面前並獲得或支付所在位置的錢
>附屬行動  如果兩個圓片都解鎖才能使用雙倍行動，否則雙倍行動圖示也只能執行單次，正常建築如果執行附屬行動都只能執行單次即使解鎖，倒退火車軌可倒退到車站，但一定要能倒退才能執行
>買牛 根據你的牛仔數目再根據圖板下方選擇，自由分配要用幾個牛仔跟多少錢買對應的卡，每個牛仔也可用來捕兩張牛卡，牛仔不會花掉
>移除災難 支付上面金額移除圖板一個對應災難放到你面前
C 卡牌補滿 如果手牌小於上限則補到上限，不夠補個人棄牌洗允重抽
*目標卡  在手牌時可在A階段或B階段任意完整行動間打出獲得左上獎勵，遊戲最後完成目標獲得右下分數，沒完成扣分數，每張的需求要分開算，遊戲結束沒打出的可選擇要不要計分，打出的則一定要計分
*遊戲在有人觸發推工作標記過紅線時，該玩家保留該標記所有其他玩家在一回合後遊戲結束，不能再加新的工人
*最後每五元一分，圖板個人建築拿右上分數，上方城市相鄰圓片拿中間分數，車站分數，收集的災難板塊上面分數，牛卡上分數，目標卡分數，自己拿到的鐵路大師板塊分數，工人列到最後兩排分數，個人圖板圓片清空分數，工作標記擁有者額外兩分，分數最高獲勝

RATING: 9
After 1 two-player game:

Rules - I read the entire rule book word for word (which I normally don't do, skipping the examples and detailed explanations of various symbols); on one hand I felt that it was necessary for me to really understand the game but at the same time the more I read the more excited I got so it was very enjoyable. In the end we forgot a few rules: replacing station master tiles with your own workers, moved our train to the delivery city during KC sub-phase 5, and chose where the hazard/teepees were placed rather than putting them in the lowest costing space. My method of teaching the rules was to first simulate how Kansas City works so they had an idea of what they were trying to achieve throughout the trail-phase. Then I picked up each neutral tile and explained the local actions and used this as a frame to explain the various other mechanisms like the cattle deck, job market, buildings, objective cards etc.


Gameplay - At two players it was fast paced and engaging. We are playing in tomorrow with 4 and I will update this review once we play. There were a couple times where I was struck with AP due to calculating how to rid myself of money in 2 turns so I wouldn't pay player 2 in the 3rd turn. I can see that there are a lot of strategies you could employ in this game that will depend on the toss of your tiles as well as how your rival ranchers will play as well. Lots of depth and strategic flow while limiting analysis. Interaction between players feels perfect; it's a cut-throat business but at the same time we're all just trying to survive.

Playtime - Took me 30 minutes to explain the rules. Another 2 hours to complete the game with two visits to the rulebook for clarification. With the same person I can easily see us wrapping this game up in just over an hour. Will edit this to comment on our 4-player game.

Theme - The theme is there but you do have to fill in the gaps I think in some places. The way you move your train while traveling through the trail but not when you actually get to Kansas City to deliver your cows is... odd? Maybe someone can explain it to me as I have never been into this genre. For this I have imagined that you are "laying down" your private railroad or rubbing elbows with the right people whenever you move your train forward. And then when you get to KC your connections (both physically and socially) can move you as far as x but after that you have to pay a freelance cowboy to take your cattle the rest of the way while you return to Texas. Other than that everything else seems great considering it's a euro.

Components - The wooden pieces are okay. The train and cattleman are adorable shapes. The board itself I personally really love. The trail feels treacherous yet beautiful. Everything is really clear on the board which I appreciate because sitting at on end of the table can make some symbols hard to see. The cattle cards are simply amazing.

Music Suggestion - We played the Red Dead Redemption sound track during this game and I would also recommend Nym's albums Convex and Warmblooded Lizard, maybe trim a song or two that don't fit). 

Overall - I love, love, love this game. Easily my top 10 games bu
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Better than Mombasa, at least for two players.  I usually can't play games more than three times before I get bored of them but this one I can see going a little longer. Set up of building tiles will be in a different order every game and that is definitely what keeps it fresh.   Your game scores can vary wildly. Once I had 98 points and then the next game I only had 57. It all depends on the set up. Only downside is it's going to take a while to absorb all the icons so your early games are going to run at least 3 to 4 hours.  

Definitely could have used a player aid for icons because I was running through the rulebook back-and-forth looking for all sorts of little rules that were hidden in small bubbles. Had to print out a bgg icon aid.  It's also very easy to miss important rules like when you do actions on the tile, you don't have to do them in left to right order.  Played three games already with this rule wrong. 

After three games we have managed to get it down to 2 1/2 hours for two players,  but that doesn't include set up. Pfister has been hit and miss with me.  Really disliked Isle of skye,  got tired of Mombasa after three plays, love port royale. GWT is definitely my favorite of the lot.  The theme is nonoffensive this time compared to mombassa which I actually had trouble trading away because of people not being happy with the theme.  

The only other negatives I can really think of are the four side tracks on the board that lead to Indian teepees and through the hazards.   This seems like a wasted space. I really don't see the point of these and why you would be building in there since it's going to cost you a fortune to get through paying all the hazards to reach your building tile.  Last game I tried building on a hazard trail once.  I managed to reach my building tile one time for free, and after that there were 4 hazards showing up on the trail,  and the building was completely useless, even when only one hazard tile was there.. Hazards might be clear for a brief time but they quickly fill in again.   I also see limited use for craftsmen.  In all three games so far no one has ever gone over two craftsmen  or built more than three buildings.   The average is two buildings. They seem underpowered compared to cowboys and engineers.  Maybe this is one of those "hard to make it work" game mechanisms like in Mombasa when you try to use the book track.  That was one thing I found really jarring in mombassa. You had this fairly smooth game, and then you had the book track,  which presented a whole new area of excessive fiddlyness.

Update after three plays: down from 8 to 6.  Lost its luster. Game takes too long to play and weakness of the craftsmen strategy is not helping. Wife also doesn't care for it at all due to distaste for western theme, so it doesn't get played.

RATING: 8
Great Western Trail is another unique take on deck-building. Here, you start out with a hand of cows, and you move your pawn along a forking path, stopping at various buildings. By the end of the path, you hope to have as many different cows as possible in your hand, which you turn in for cash and other rewards, and then start the whole process again from the beginning of the trail. As the game progresses, players add more and more buildings to the path, which allows them to do more manipulation. Additionally, each player has a player board from which discs can be removed to reveal extra abilities, Hansa Teutonica-style. The game is certainly very unique, and has a lot of variability as well as strategies to explore. There is a bit of card draw luck, but on the whole I found the game pretty intriguing.

(4 plays)

RATING: 6.5
A beautiful board, loads of praise, a lot of mechanisms and interesting choices. Or? Most of it is true, but the game feels weirdly scripted, the first few trips to Kansas City seem exactly the same, and all players seem to do the same actions. It just feels like the game is playing you, not the other way around. Somewhere in the middle it starts picking up, you have some better cows, and the deck building feels somewhat exiting, but the buildings and the train movement just feel flat. And then it ends. For a moderately long game (on average around 2,5 hours), the end just feels abrupt and anti-climactic. It's a hard thing to admit, because I expected to love this game, it's just not my idea of fun.

Positive points are that the rounds go quick, although the rules take some time to explain. It is beautiful, and the deck building is good after a while.

RATING: 9
A great Euro style engine builder that seems simple on the surface yet the simplicity betrays a sincere depth. Multiple avenues of victory present themselves without any single avenue working better than others. The only downside is that others in my gaming circle feel this is complicated and takes a long time, though I personally disagree with both of those sentiments. Others may not like the cyclical nature of the game board where you literally run laps around the board over and over again. Ultimately, this is a great game that deserves 3-4 plays just to see the options and avenues. I would recommend this without reservation.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I've never understood why Mombasa got so much appreciation as it did because frankly I thought it was unfocused and mechanically overwrought. Consequently I was slightly apprehensive regarding this game as it is designed by the same designer, produced by the same publisher, and has a game weight on par with the aforementioned title. As it turns out, my suspicions were confirmed but only subjectively speaking. Great Western Trail really is a good heavy euro with multiple paths to victory, adequate variability in the setup to guarantee a different game experience each run-through, and enough apparent identifiers in the decision space to secure the possibility for the players to ultimately master the game. But in my own subjective opinion, heavily biased (as opinions usually are) by my personal game palate, I find Great Western Trail to be yet another near miss from Alexander Pfister because of its, for lack of a better term, "hodge-podgeness". I would describe it as a frankensteinian game design, where Caylus, Russian Railroads and Dominion have been stripped for parts and put together again, not higgledy-piggledy per se, but neither sleek nor harmonious, and then finally brought to life. The game absolutely works and is unquestionably an impressive design feat, but its monstrosity is still showing.

RATING: 8
(01/18)
It is the quickest long game I've played, turns are so quick, time goes by faster with this one.

and yet another game with a Dominion-like mechanism.

There's a lot to think, there's a lot to do, there's a lot of luck, but ways to mitigate it.

I really suck at this game, but i like it.

I think one of the things against this game is the teaching, there's a lot to cover, I compare it to a Feast for Odin, FFO is easy to teach, there's a lot, but it is really the same actions but bigger, in GWT you have to go through a lot of stuff, trains, people, cows, hazards, teepes, green hand and black hand, stations, stations where you can put your workers in, white spots, black and white spots, red X, building you can build... etc. In FFO you can explain each row of actions and say "you need to feed and cover this, to make more points you need more territory or building, this is how you hunt, this is how you pillage" basically and people get the hang of it organically, GWT has to be taught in detail

RATING: 8
Another of Alexander Pfister's "a lot of simple" games: there is nothing ground-breaking or complicated about GWT, but the many parts work well together to create an engaging game where, with each iteration you feel like you can do better next time round. The teach, much like Mombasa, is a bit of a bear; and, although I'll concede that it is somewhat thematic (breeding cattle) I hate it when deck-builders don't give you the card you just bought straight away (BH2045 has a lot of good stuff to answer for in that respect). There is a some player interaction with the buildings, but for the most part you compete with yourself to improve your various engines. Although I'm not usually one for a lot player interaction and denial, I think I prefer Mombasa slightly in this respect, as there is a lot of push and pull on the map, co-optition for the shares and outright panic getting to your preferred actions. On the plus side, no Bookkeeping track in GWT!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game is a giant roundel where players circle around trying to get the highest value hand at the end of the loop, consisting of a set all different cow cards. As you play, your movement points get used up more and more, as players add additional buildings and hazard tiles to the routes. Which buildings you construct and how slow or fast you cycle around defines your whole game, which is some fascinating and ever changing decision points. There's a degree of deck building happening as well, as the cow cards come from and are discarded to a personal deck. Many of the actions allow you to manipulate that deck in different ways, removing low value cows or adding more high value cows.

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:star: Multiple plays stay interesting
:star: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::star::star::nostar: Raw enjoyment

RATING: 8
Great Western Trail has a lot of things going for it that I enjoy. It rewards long term planning and allows for planning several moves ahead. There is a good bit of engine building which I enjoy. There is a theme that ties the game together (although it admittedly is a bit loose and isn't as finely integrated as I think most Rosenbergs are). It's made by a designer that I think makes quite a few smart games. 

While I don't have enough plays to confirm it, I suspect there the various strategies and paths are viable, and a quick search of the BGG threads suggests that it is possible to win with myriad of strategies including focusing on cattle, trains, or buildings. The winning strategy often will change based on how the craftsmen emerge and how the buildings are placed, along with what choices the other players make. All of this leads to a game with some great strategic depth that will reward repeated plays. 

Taken all together this is the sort of game I often love. I quite like this one and further plays may push this even higher for me. In a sign that this game really is something great for me, I often keep thinking about it after my play, considering what choices I could have improved upon and what strategies I can employ in the future. Looking forward to playing this some more and exploring what it has to offer.

RATING: 10
This game made me aware of the works of Alexander Pfister, and after 14 games I would say, this is currently my number 1 game of all time! This game is just perfect, but here are some reasons why I like it that much:
- The theme! I'm not a thematic gamer, so this criteria doesn't weight that much, but a great and not overused theme is definately a plus for me. This is such, especially for a heavy eurogame. 
- The job market: it is similar to the marketplace (??) in Mombasa, where you have to always keep your eyes on it, and manage your advance on the trail accordingly. When you arrive to Kansas City has uttermost importance. 
- The game is both strategic and tactical!!! There is usually a general path you take (builder, engineer or cowboy) but then you have to react to what your opponents are doing. The mix of this creates so much tension!!
- As a sidenote: compared to the popular views here, I don't think that you have to make a strategic decision about your play at the very beginning. IMHO you have to decide later, when you have to decide about the direction of your specialization. This decision, alongside the timing of it creates much tension during the game. 
The ever changing rondel (?) mechanic is fantastic. The tactical placement of your buildings, the alternative routes, the agonizing decisions about which actions you take and which to leave behind in the actual round is terrific. 
- The art is fantastic!!!

RATING: 10
My first experience with this game was off-the-charts amazing.  It was the single funnest gaming experience I've had to date.

As I was playing, I found myself caught up in my choices to such a degree that I neither cared about the points nor what time it was.  I know I didn't win the game, but I was soooo far from caring because I was enjoying what I was doing so much!

It's extremely satisfying.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Thoughts after one game:

- It had a good overall feel and there was a lot to explore.
- There are many ways to 'chain together' benefits.
- It is an enjoyable romp with minor 'gotcha' moments that are good fun. But interaction is surprisingly low for this kind of 'Rube Goldberg Machine' type game.
- It's novel how rapidly the turns come around ... although you do very little in them and I felt the frequency made play more reactive.
- Despite its long game length and supposedly numerous strategic options, it felt like all four players were going through roughly the same motions.
- You have no idea who's winning. The game is a huge 'points salad' and, unless you're a math guru, the scores aren't known until you use your calculator and notepad at the end.
- Your choices are too dictated by the luck of your hand draw. If your hand is poor, you'll go to buildings that change your hand etc.
- Likewise, the restrictions on movement distance/direction mean you'll tend to hop forward to whichever of your one or two buildings ahead will do something useful. It's not rocket science.
- There's not really enough 'open information' to play a truly cutthroat Euro game, as you can't know what is in other players' hands. They might be desperate to go to one place or to a completely different place on the board. (On the plus-side, you can see their 'objective cards', which gives you something to try to hinder.)

Overall, it's a good game BUT it pretends to be a delightfully crunchy Euro, like ones by Vital Lacerda etc, and it isn't. The big decisions are too obvious and the information is too hidden. It 'feels' more like a long Roll For The Galaxy to me, even though mechanically it's very, very different. 

When you lose it'll probably be because of things like failing to check you have enough money before heading to a location, or failing to remember what all the buildings do.

This is the kind of game where people will argue it's barely luck dependent, as they're deluded into thinking the game allows smart players to mitigate the luck. Then they lose due to bad luck.

So it's a game you can certainly 'discover' over many plays, but I think it'll be a bit more Stodge City than Dodge City.

RATING: 5
Remember when euros revolutionized boardgames by maximizing game depth with a minimum amount of rules and mechanics? 

Yeah. Those days are long gone. The era of less is more is fading from the collective memory of the hobby. We are in an era of heavy euros where more is more. More actions. More things to convert. More tokens, More tracks. More cards. More ways to score points. And most of all, more icons. Icons with cubes. Icons with arrows. Icons in every color. Icons. 

Great Western Trail is a modern heavy euro in all it's iconic excess. And let's not forget a near-total absence of interaction. It's the kind of game where you can start your turn while the previous player is halfway through his because nothing he does will affect your plans. 

The placement of buildings on the trail, reminiscent of Caylus, works well. The deck building with the cattle is nice. But those merits don't save Great Western Trail from being another grinding, needlessly complex, multiplayer-solitaire chore of a point salad.

RATING: 9.17
This game is AMAZING! It lives up to my expectations and then some. I want to play it a couple more times before I solidify it in my rankings, but it even has a chance to become #1 of all time for me. There is so much to do in this game and so many possible routes to victory. However, this game is not as complicated to play as some reviewers have made it sound. There's just a lot to explain, but it is not complicated to do (easy to play, tough to master). I do feel that a person's opinion of deck building will have a good deal to do with your starting ranking for this game, but it's not the only thing you're doing. So, if you hate deck building, this is still not a deal breaker. The replayability is fantastic even from the start if you randomize the building sides and placement to use (for starting buildings and the player's buildings). I think my favorite part of the game is just the number of times you get to go through the trail and deliver to KC. It's a sense of accomplishment and you really feel like you are getting somewhere and doing something. It gives the same tough decisions of a euro game without that feeling like you are slogging through the game slowly for the elusive victory points.

Get it! Play it! Brilliant!

RATING: 8
Gostei bastante do GWT. Do ponto de vista do game design, é incrível que um jogo desse tamanho quase não tenha upkeep. Isso faz com que ele flua rápido, sem interrupções ao longo da partida para fazer manutenção, o que aumenta muito a imersão, não no tema, mas no jogo em si.
A mecânica do rondelzão disfarçado que vai se construindo ao longo do jogo se integra super bem com o deck building e faz com que cada partida seja bem diferente, só com o setup inicial. Talvez eu não tenha me divertido tanto quanto o brilhantismo do jogo sugere, provavelmente pq sou menos fã de jogos pesados e com muitas regras. Mas esse é muito bom.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.3
[u]The Positive[/u]
(+) The core mechanics of this game are rock solid. I wouldn't call the different mechanics (deck building, point to point movement, some engine building in abilities and personal actions, minor set collection, ...) especially innovative, but they are brought together in an exceptionally good way in order to create an intersting game of which the gameplay feels familiar, yet fresh. The game engine runs smoothly and makes for interesting decision making all of the time. During play the board changes with certain paths for your cowboy moving to the Kansas market becoming more or less attractive. The creation of additional buildings along the path creates opportunities for a few players, but slows others down - which they can mitigate by upgrading their ability to move their cowboy further.

(+) Some will disagree with me on this, but for what clearly is a euro-game, I found this game having its theme sufficiently shine trough its mechanics. This aids in understanding the logic of the game and makes for a fun and interesting experience: while making decisions you feel like you are a cowboy optimizing his herd or finding the best location to invest in a building, not just moving tiles and markers around. It is very thematic that when placing buildings I am making a trade-off between placing it on the main road or on a temptingly short but treacherous path with potential hazards impeding the way. With 4 (to 6) cow-cards in hand you navigate yourself a way to the market in Kansas and along the way you try to make the best of it with the cows you are travelling with (with some (I admit often unthematic) opportunities to optimize your hand before reaching the market). Investing in staff feels thematic: when you invest in cowboys, you get better choices when buying new cows; investing in craftsmen (I call them builders) allows you to quickly build or upgrade to spectacular buildings and hiring engineers will boost your railroad endeavours, allowing you to invest in the most interesting stations.

(+) This game is a point salad in that tons of things score you points (money, buildings, deliveries, stations, removed tiles, cows, goal cards, specialized staff, ...), but it doesn't feel like a 'dry' point salad, as you are clearly building up to things. Different strategies are possible (focus on the right herd and making great deliveries [gives you points and serious income coupled with flexibility during actions], focus on buildings [gives you points, slows opponents down and grants you powerful actions not available to opponents], focus on stations [can get you many points, allows for quick action upgrades and enhances income]). You're in it for the points, but each point scoring act also benefits you in some other way (increased income, engine building on your personal abilties/actions, slowing opponents down, opening new powerful building actions, clearing a path of strategic relevance of hazards, ...). That's why this point salad doesn't have that classic 'point salad feel': more often than not, you are not just doing something only for the points, you are also doing them because they help you in other ways. Of course, this only holds true until about 75% of the game: in the finale the point salad aspects do break through with players calculating the worth of their final possible moves in victory points and the game becoming a bit more abstract and mathematical - but hey, you are playing a euro-game still, and we are loving that, don't we? It is a big achievement that for the biggest part of the game, these mathematical aspects often remain hidden beneath a wonderful veil of theme and a feeling of 'personal progress'.

(+) Players start of weak: few personal actions, no personal buildings, a very deplorable herd of cows, ... While playing, players are constantly improving all those things, which gives a satisfying feeling of growth. For this reason this game has a positive vibe: players can constantly improve themselves. The game engine constantly spawns positive dilemmas: "Do I go for this great upgrade, or do I invest in this potential golden gooze(/cow ;) )?".

(+) Different strategic lines can be followed, and I get the impression it is not necessary to focus on everything (despite everyting scoring points). As a player you are in control, and you are free to make choices on which goals you want to (or should) persue - the game will probably not penalize you (much) for neglecting a certain aspect. After having played, the game keeps you thinking about different things you could have tried and other ways to build up your possibilities. Replayability is guaranteed because of the different set-up each time (which makes assessing the board from the start of the game important!): every game players play with a different combination of buildings, the order in which the neutral buildings come out impact play, some paths may be more attractive than others at the start of the game due to the random distribution of hazards, ... 

(+) The game art is colorful and fun. It looks inviting when displayed on the table and the component quality is very good (however, some might say that it is a shame that your personal tableau is thick paper and not cardboard and there is an unfortunate misprint on [u][url=https://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/1659174/red-player-building-8-misprint]tile 8B which will be corrected by the publishers[/url][/u] - but don't let that scare you off as I'm sure this will get fixed and in 95% of your games you won't be needing this tile).



[u]The Negative[/u]
(+/-) Players seeking a healthy balance between well performed stategies being rewarded and some luck-elements to keep things spicy and interesting will love this game as it provides just that: the player who realizes his plans in the best and most efficient way will probably win, but occasionally something unexpected can happen (with a card or tile draw). I'm one of those weirdos who would have liked this healthy balance to have tilted even more towards strategy, but I'm well aware that I will be an exception in this. I will say that this game is slightly more luck-based than Mombasa, the previous design by Pfister and to me personally, that's a shame. Don't take me wrong: this is definitely is a skill-based game, not a luck-based one (as the matter of fact, I have a victory ratio of 100% so far :whistle: ), but still it can be a little annoying that a wonderfully ingenious plan can not get realized due to that one impossible draw.

(-) The game truly invites players to make long-term investments: empowering your personal actions, buying (expensive!!) sensational cows [i][who would have thought I would ever write such a sentence on a public forum?!][/i], investing in staff in order to achieve more powerful actions, building impressive buildings, ... There are so much goodies to be had, but this build-up is slow (most of the time you will be investing money into building up an engine and you won't have money left to let it run right away) and before you know it, the game is over. This game had the potential to be a great engine builder, but it is too short to get a truly satisfying experience from running your engine: a lot of your hard fought for investments will only pay off one or two times. I understand this design choice: probably this was necessary to avoid dominant stategies or avoid run-away-leader problems, but still I feel it is a shame that all those beautifull engine building aspects are there, but I can't take that to the maximum due to game length. This makes playing 'Great Western Trail' feel like a great party, but the music stops when everybody is finally on the dance floor.

(-) Gameplay is smooth and quick most of the time, but at times the game is prone to analysis paralysis, especially when a suitable location for buildings must be found or a player with big plans for a sequence of turns runs low on money and tries to find a way to getting his stuff done (calculating expenses and income). This is especially true as the game is nearing its end and the detailed point calculation starts. Still, this is a minor complaint here, because overall turns go quick because most of the time players can prepare their turn while opponents are taking theirs. 

(-) The core of a game turn is very simple: Move, perform an action and fill up your hand with cows until you are back to your max hand. Easy and elegant. However, there is lots of iconography to learn and teach about and new players struggle when rules about different ways to score keep coming (especially true with game rules relating to the railroad).


[u]Conclusion[/u]
In 2015 we got introduced to designer Alexander Pfister by playing Mombasa, a game I (and a major part of the gaming community) hold in very high regard. As the matter of fact, I like Mombasa so much that 'Great Western Trail' was a sure buy for me at this year's Spiel fair. I haven't regretted that decision for a second, because just like Mombasa, with 'Great Western Trail', Pfister again created a wonderful and clever euro-game which faces players with an interesting challenge to build up towards a satisfying goal, keeping players on the edge of their seat from start to finish. Similar to Mombasa, the possibilities for build-up  in 'Great Western Trail' are all challenging and slow - but I would say that 'Great Western Trail' is the ligher of these two games.

For the rest, these two games play out totally differently and they don't feel similar at all. I have witnessed electric discharge above the board of Mombasa due to brainpower spinning out of control, and I don't expect such scenario's with 'Great Western Trail', which also offers much interesting decision making, but plays more frivolously. The mechanics work differently and they each have their own unique flavor and dynamic. Due to the fact that 'Great Western Trail' plays a little more lightly compared to 'Mombasa', but still remains an interesting challenge, I can see this title hitting the table a little more frequently and with a broader spectrum of players.

What I mainly like about this game is the smooth integration of some great known mechanics, creating a dynamic of growth during play and giving the game its own character. It is a positive game full of interesting positive choices. It is a major accomplishment of the designer to have designed an excellent balanced point salad game leaving open enough options for players to persue their own strategic plans, but which doesn't have the dry flavor many other classical point salad games have. This is due to the fact that the game couples nice advantages to point-scoring moves, ensuring the game keeps telling a story other than calculus.

The game offers an appealing toy-box for the engine builders amoung us, but be aware that they should not get their hopes up too much as the game might be over before you ran your engine sufficiently. The game sticks with you in a way that long after playing you find yourself thinking about the best way to navigate through the canyons and what great opportunities lie in getting those buildings out or hiring a different kind of staff next time - and that's all the more reason for me to stick with this game!


[u]Tips for those going in[/u]
(*) Money is tight, especially in the beginning of the game. Investing everyting in building an engine you can't put to use due to a lack of money might not be the best short term plan
(*) In my experience, it works well to put some focus on a strategy, and after you got that up and running, usually as a side benefit, you can start investing in a second strategic path. For example: if you invest in your herd (better cows, deck optimization, ...) you will perform well on deliveries, generating points. However, you will also increase your income, which you could invest in hiring the right staff, for example to build better buildings - opening a new strategic path for your game. Don't try to invest in your herd, buildings, train, ... all at once: best take it one step at a time.
(*) Getting your personal actions/abilities upgraded soon is very intersting. You can do this by making an early delivery or by trying to snatch away the early stations. I especially love the auxiliary ability to replace one or two cow cards in your hand.
(*) Staff is expensive, but very powerful. In some rare occasions they will be cheaper to hire: when that happens, you will want to be prepared to snatch them away before the others do! You can not hope to perform well on a main strategy (herd+delivery, buildings, stations) without the right staff (respectively: cowboys, crafsmen, engineers).
(*) Timing is important: will the game get rushed by players racing again and again to Kansas city, or will there be more time for you to build things up with players taking more stops in order to enjoy builing actions?
(*) Try to avoid stiff penalties for making bad deliveries: they will eat away your end game score and your income.

RATING: 7
Good medium weight Euro. There’s no a whole lot of player interaction, apart from the pacing and some competition over worker tiles, bonus tiles on the railroad branches, etc. The theme is fun, but it really is just a mish-mash of Euro mechanisms plastered on to the setting that don’t make a lot of thematic sense. I kind of wish there was more focus on different paths to Kansas City, that all paths involved going through the wilderness to get to waystations with buildings as opposed to being able to hop building to building the whole way to Kansas - where’s the cattle drive part. The best mechanics is definitely the deck building/hand management trying to construct a hand that will maximize your income and chip placement eligibility on the railroad when you get to Kansas City; this affects whether you try to move fast because you have a good hand, making the game end faster, or go slow to have more time to reconstruct your hand. I also wish you cared more about what buildings your opponents built, like Caylus/LoW. Not sure why the Indians pay you to take them off the board. Are you getting paid a bounty to kill them, but you have to pay a penalty if you want to kill the 3 Indian tiles in the higher track? Probably reading too much into it, but this just show how the theme is just a setting for a framework of well-integrated Euro mechanisms; the game is not trying to actually simulate a cattle drive.

No Solitario
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The Big Game of 2017 for me, much as Russian Railroads was in 2014. We are playing this to death right now, and as of this typing, I haven't burned out yet. But I could see it.

Exactly the sort of design I usually despise (a mish-mash of mechanics thrown against the wall, in the hopes that they stick). For whatever reason, this time it worked for me.

Great Western is a (cleverly-disguised) rondel game, where you are going through a loop with some choices about where to stop during the loop. This part reminds me very much of Istanbul, which I consider one of the great designs of the last few years. GWT allows you to modify the loop however, adding stops along the way that only you can use (and that serve to hamper the progress of your opponents).

It has a hand-management/deck-building element, pretty much a rip of Dominion like all of these knock-offs. That's fine.

It has a variable set-up that truly does make every game different. Again, like Istanbul in this way.

It has an over-arching strategic template that reminds me much of Russian Railroads. There are a limited number of kind of "tracks" that you can pursue, and you need to pick probably two, and then develop them well, to play well.

For me and my sons (as well as our game group) it has been a puzzle that we are really enjoying trying to figure out. It is also without question going to be a tournament-worthy game, and will be featured at the tourney-based conventions that we like to attend (WBC, PrezCon, EuroQuest). UPDATE: I'm the GM of this at PrezCon now, and possibly at WBC and EuroQuest in the near future as well.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Less would've been more!

Perhaps better than Mombasa, but my wife hates it - for good reason: Like Mombasa, great ideas and mechanics, an interesting theme. However, instead of streamlining the game, complex layer was put upon layer. The game is inelegant and at times outright confusing. And again, theme and mechanics don't gel together. I sell cows in KC, yet they come back out of nowhere. If I sell along the path, another cow teleports to me. Wow! But we're obviously in an alternate universe anyhow, since San Francisco is off to the East. (Historically correct would've been shipping from Dodge City to Eastern markets. And BTW, the Southern Pacific was built from San Francisco towards El Paso, not the other way around!)
But as I said: better than Mombasa (a very good game), except maybe GWT is even more 'points salad'. Final scoring easily takes 10 minutes. Why, o why? 
Did I mention the icons and rule book are below par? Details - of which there are like a hundred - are near impossible look up in the rulebook. I *really* don't like the development process at Eggert these days... 
The other Pfister games (those with Pelikan in particular) are more fluid and to my liking. GWT and Mombasa could - and should! - have been perfect, but unfortunately transformed into some mutant. Still very good, but an opportunity missed.

PS. I don't see similarities to Caylus and Russian Railroads! All three are excellent, thinky, heavy, and unfortunately overly long games. 
But other than that, GWT feels original and very different from the other two. They may LOOK similar, but work out in very different paths...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Great Western Trail has a bit of everything. The game board is a giant rondel you build as you play, there is deck building, and there are some engine building elements. 

There are three main strategic approaches to the game corresponding to the 3 main types of workers. Strategies built around cowboys involve buying better and better cows for end game points and to set up big deliveries. Craftsmen allow you to build buildings that can simultaneously give you stronger action options, slow down/tax your opponents, and give you points. Buildings cannot be your main source of points, but can contribute to an overall strategy. Engineers let you move your train and get to stations.

I expected to like Great Western Trail quite a bit more than I actually do. The issue is that players are encouraged to rush the end of the game, and I never feel like I have enough time to execute interesting strategic approaches. I haven't had much success with engineers, and getting the most and highest value deliveries is simply more important than other action efficiency considerations unless you can make up the points by getting to the high value stations.

RATING: 10
Really great game. Those who dislike it might call it a "mish-mash of old mechanics" but I'd say it's an "exciting blend of tried and trusted mechanics"! There's elements of Caylus (neutral buildings) Monopoly(!) (fines on opponent buildings), deck-building, and that rail track is visually reminiscent of Russian Railroads. Surprisingly easy to teach and play for what is a comparatively heavy Euro. Several different strategies present themselves, and their efficacy will vary with the distribution of cards and tiles which emerge. Probably a difficult game to master. Great theme - another winner from this excellent designer!

RATING: 7.5
Some things about GWT don't make a Great Deal of Sense: the idea that the cattle you deliver is all about diversity rather than volume; the idea that as ranchers you build buildings on the trail; the whole conceit of Local versus Auxiliary actions. It's kind of silly in many respects.

But it's also an involved Euro where you have that Rosenberg-esque feeling of reward for each turn, yet with faster play. GWT plays really rapidly - even on a first play, after the first few turns things speed up and before you know it, time is running out. If you can overlook the thematic idiosyncrasies then you might get a real kick out of this one.

RATING: 8
[2018-01-28]
Bár csak fél parti sikerült elsőre így is bőven meg lehetett ismerni a Nagy Wester Utazást. A játékosok marhákat visznek eladni, közben az odavezető több lehetséges útvonalon akciókat hajtanak végre, hogy mire a piacra érkeznek a legkülönbözőbb, nagy értékű haszonállatokkal rendelkezzenek. A játék a jól bevált recepttel működik, több mechanizmust gyúr egymásba. Az egyik fő, már ismert a pakliépítés. Tetszett, mert jól működik, célunk kiszórni a paklinkból a gyenge marhákat és helyükre pontot hozó, minél változatosabb új egyedeket szerezzünk. Saját táblánkon akcióhelyeket tudunk felszabadítani az egyes szállítmányok leszállításával. Ezen akciók közül a legtöbb nem túl erős, a fő táblán jóval használhatóbbakat találni a feltett semleges, majd saját épületek által. Saját táblánkon még három segítőt is gyűjthetünk, ezek közül az egyik a marhavásárlásokat segíti, másikuk az épületeink felrakáshoz szükséges, harmadikuk pedig a vonatunk előrejutásában, ugyanis ez is szükséges a marhaeladáshoz. Számomra a fő táblán található lépés, akcióválasztás tetszett a legjobban. Alapesetben négy lépéslehetőségünk van, a táblán még csak semleges épületek figyelnek a játék elején, ezeken tudunk max. ennyi lépést megtenni, tehát mi döntjük el melyik akción állunk meg és hajtjuk végre. Idővel saját épületeink is felkerülnek, amin csak mi magunk tudunk megállni és esetlegesen ellenfeleink pénzt adnak nekünk áthaladásukkor. Vannak elágazások amelyeken nehézségekkel találhatjuk szemben magunkat, de lehet néha megéri arra menni, hogy kevesebb vámot kelljen fizetünk, illetve ne játékostársunknak, hanem inkább a banknak. Ezek a nehézségek a játék során gyűlhetnek, illetve el is tűnhetnek, amivel egy-egy útvonal vonzósága folyamatosan változhat. A nehézségek útvonalára épített épületek még plusz bónuszt is adnak az alapakcióinkon felül. Nem tudom ez mennyire egyedi, de nagyon jól működik és ez adja a játék sava borsát. Rettentő érdekes, hogy az elején még kevés akciólehetőség miatt mindenhol megállunk, később, ahogy egyre több akció válik elérhetővé egyre gyorsabban haladunk át a táblán, hogy a már egyre jobb állatokból álló kezünket gyorsabban eladhassuk a piacon.
A játék sikere megérdemelt, nagyon jól összegyúrták, még a tematika is átjön, komoly euró. Egyetlen negatívumot tudok mondani, az pedig a játék hossza. Szabállyal 2 és fél óra alatt a játék felééig jutottunk. Véleményem szerint nem gyorsul a játék később se, ezért egy nagyon hosszú játékidejű játékról beszélhetünk. Nem véletlen a közösség ajánlása, hogy 3 fővel ideális a társas, 4-en brutál hosszú lehet.

RATING: 8
The issue with this game is the buildings.  You can completely skip building any buildings, race through the game, and win in a 2 player game.  All you need to do is get a building out in a location that forces people to pay you cash.  You go for money, buy cows, sell cows, and discard weak cows.  You will eventually  just race to Kansas and if you lap the other players just once you will earn more cash than the other players allowing you to buy more cows than the other players maximizing points.  I buy cowboys and nothing else.  I still have fun playing the game but the other player is going to be disappointed with how fast the game ends because you moved the  game to end  quickly.  I would like to see the 2 player game fixed.  I do really enjoy this game with 3 players so this is why I give it an 8.  I want to play it anytime with 4 players but rarely with 2 or 3.

RATING: 9
Rightfully received a ton of buzz at Essen 2016. This is just the kind of game that blends a bunch of mechanics together and does it right. There's several things to do, and each time you take your herd through to Kansas City, you have to decide carefully your sequence of actions. By building buildings to determine the actions and when you can take them, the replayability is huge. There's a bit of a set collection and managing your the herd and gathering more cattle is a cool deckbuilding mechanic. On top of all of this, you've got this Egizia-esque mechanc of how far do you go down the trail to Kansas City, knowing you can't go back your next round. The bonuses are varied, and it just seems that you're immersed for a couple of hours making tough and interesting choices.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
As ridiculous as it sounds, the only thing that keeps me from giving this game a perfect score is the initial learning curve and /literally/ the worst board game instructions manual I've ever read.

Otherwise this is currently my group's absolute favorite game. Engaging, fast, and incredibly diverse in gameplay. There are many, many different avenues to victory and viable strategies, from monopolizing train stations, to amassing cleared hazards, to buildings, to worker monopolization, and of course, good ole' cow-hustlin'.

The game starts very simply, but as it continues and your own power grows, it turns into snakes and ladders /from hell/. But no matter how late in the game it gets, and how complex the board may look, no one's turn ever takes more than 10 seconds, and it will often be your turn again almost immediately after taking your last one. There's no snore-fest downtime in this game. It's a race to Kansas City through-and-through, but prepare your herd well, or you'll never get to send your cows off to the coveted San Francisco.

At the time of this writing, this is (in my opinion) the best and most fun boardgame ever made.

GET. THIS. GAME.

---
[url=https://www.boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/217189/item/5043233]1 partial play[/url]

This is a pretty cool deck-building style of game. Your deck has a bunch of types of cattle. You're trying to optimize your hand in the time (several turns) it takes to get your marker from start to Kansas City. Along the way, you can stop at buildings (neutral ones, and those you build yourself) to take a variety of actions (e.g., buy more cattle, earn coins, thin your deck). At the end of your cattle run, the higher the value of your hand the further you can place your marker (worth more VP, earn more coins). 

Once you understand the rules and the functions of the various buildings, the game SHOULD play very quickly. Very fun game, worth getting a copy!

RATING: N/A
2-4, 75-150m
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Such a great game, my second favorite of 2016 (behind the amazing Scythe).  This game is kind of hard to put into buckets based on mechanics.  It is basically a rondel game with multiple paths through the route on each pass.  Each time it is your turn you move a number of steps up to your movement speed and use the action for the space you end your movement on.  These can be many different things from hiring workers to acquiring wealth to rustling cattle.  There is also a minor deck building element.  At the end of each pass of the route you gain benefits based on the value of the cattle cards in your hand.

I think my favorite thing about the game is the sheer depth and breadth both of strategy it offers.  There are so many ways to gather points and many paths and decisions to be made at every point of the game.  Even little things like being able to choose whether or not to play objective cards in front of you or keep them in your deck offer interesting tidbits of strategy.  Playing them in front of you means you can lose points if they aren't achieved.  You can still score them while in your deck but they also serve to clutter up your hand and deck.

The components are incredibly standard to sub par.  There's really no artwork to speak of except for the worker profiles and different cows.  I never knew there were so many different kinds of cows.  Some of the actual components aren't great like the thin paper player mats and others are fine but nothing really is better than that.

But what is on offer here design wise can make you forget about the sturdiness of what you play it on.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Can I look at my cards? Rules: You can look at hand and discard, not draw.
My opinion (and others): You can also look at draw, as long as you shuffle afterwards.
Reasoning (from thread): You're allowed to look at your hand and discard pile and you know every card you added, by definition you know what is left in your deck. We could make everyone get pen/paper and keep track of this information and slow the game down, but that is just unnecessary bookkeeping. So we allow anyone to check their deck then reshuffle. This game has zero deck manipulation (putting cards back in your deck in a specific order) so it only makes sense to allow people to look whenever they want. A random draw is a random draw.
