RATING: 5
Such convolution. Literally half the game could and should have been removed. It feels like someone playtested, found an issue, added a rule to fix it, and repeated this process ad nauseam.

The game has so many things that just don't make sense. New cards going into your discard pile. The train advancing *away* from the station, but making it cheaper to run *from the station*. Green teepees. Blue teepees. Teepees at all. Oh and hazards. Which aren't the same as teepees. And goal cards, which go into your discard pile. But there's not really any great risk to them because at the end of the game you can just choose not to use some. Oh and you can build stuff. But there's really only one optimal spot to put that first level forest building. And the rest of the buildings? Well, good luck winning if you try to pursue that strategy.

Then there's the cows. Blue ones. Red ones. Yellow ones. Purple ones. Convoluted costings that I am sure were precisely mathematically calculated. But of course, selling two cows of the same breed gets you no more money. Mmkay. Oh, wait, but you can get ribbons to pretty up your cows! But you can use them on any cows. A bit like a fraudster.

The rulebook isn't the best, the iconography is mediocre to poor (Why oh why are the costs to buy workers listed as negative numbers!? Then there's that building that lets you hire workers, the second one listed as -2. So... two cheaper? No, two more expensive. Ughhhh.

Once you add all the rather disjointed elements together the game is challenging, but my god, what a chore. It's a shallow game with a very very very broad set of rules. Give me an elegant euro like CoB, or Puerto Rico any day of the week.

RATING: 6
Probably my favourite euro-efficiency game since Russian Railroads. Players move their cowboy meeple along a track representing the a cattle trail ending in Kansas City (supposedly, according to the forums, ahistorical nonsense). Initially the trail is sparsely populated by action spaces, but as players construct buildings new actions become available. 

On arrival in Kansas the player must sell their herd, scoring for each different type of cattle card in hand. A higher-scoring herd is worth more money and more victory points; a low-scoring herd is severely penalised.

It plays smoothly but I have some misgivings about its implicit constraints.

The requirement to sell in Kansas essentially prevents monofocus on other elements (such as a train push or building strategy). Likewise, the cost of delivering to a city far ahead of the player's train forces participation in the train push, and so on. There are a number of approaches and room for tactical variation (e.g. herd deck thinning vs deck cycling, pushing one scoring category vs another) but extreme strategies do not appear to be viable.

It's particularly annoying when these constraints are based on unthematic nonsense -- e.g., if you can't deliver to the same city twice because you're establishing a trade route or something, then other players shouldn't be able to deliver to it either. If there were actual competition for those slots, well, that would be interesting.

Random elements are superficially minor but can be significant. Good/bad herd draws can have particularly high impact, both immediately on scoring and on avoiding the enormous opportunity cost of wasting actions thinning or cycling a deck; other in-game randomness tends to merely inhibit strategic planning (availability of useful person tiles, unpredictability of hazards/teepees). 

I don't think turn order advantage is sufficiently compensated across all random layouts.  While the board has branching paths, some actions are often exclusive, which makes placing buildings with a cost to pass on that path extremely powerful. One particularly problematic example is if there is only one path to the "hire a person" action. This action requires significant money, so players can't go broke to avoid paying a toll, and it is required to be competitive with any strategy. A more minor niggle is that if players have roughly the same tempo then the player last in turn order will be the one to end the game and therefore will have fewer turns.

A pleasant way to pass the time but overrated.

RATING: 7
Good heavy game with two major flaws in my opinion:
The game doesn't keep track of who has how many points. So either players keep track themselves which slows down the game even more or they don't which gives the game a random feel - you just do what you do and discover at the end how well you actually did.

The deck building mechanic adds too much chance for a heavy game that takes that long. Card draw near the end of the game can result in massive point swings - in one game at one point I had a 3/4 chance for 13 points and a 1/4 chance for -6 points, a 19 point difference that hinged on drawing one card. It is possible to significantly reduce the randomness, but that limits the strategies you can use. I can see the appeal of the deck building mechanism in GWT, but I don't think it works that well.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Seeing the high ratings, this is no doubt a very intricate interlocking Euro game with dozens of different strategies and options to explore.

Yet, I pass. For several reasons:
a) Theme. Herding cow, while novel, is not a theme that grabs me. I like the gun-toting cowboys better even while they're not real-life. Carson City, BANG!, that stuff. Also, while the board might be thematic, the deckbuilding is typical abstraction that might work as a game-mechanic, but not thematically.
b) No novel mechanics. Build your own map, get somewhere first before point values are going down, deckbuilding, resource into coins into vp's, different actions etc. It might be great interlocking but where is the novelty? Sure, if you don't need novelty but simply a game that runs like a clock, this might be great. But not if you already own dozens of other games that do that.
c) Too much stuff going on. Game you need investing time in. Time I don't have.
d) Point salad. Publishers tend to take a look at the rankings of BGG and as these Feld games get high ratings the 'point salad plague' seems to have taken hold. Reward those players for ANYTHING they do! Make it so that games end 499 to 503 points so everyone will feel it was so close! Sorry, while I know basketball is a popular sport I prefer soccer. A good 0-1 will do, if the game itself is tense and interesting. Games tend to go for lower tension arcs, rewarding players in their very own turn with minor achievements, thereby avoiding the disappointment some can feel if their grand multi-turn plans are twarthed somewhere at the very last moment. Sorry guys, I WANT multi-turn planning. I WANT scheming for an action somewhere down the road and (inadvertentedly) being foiled in my schemes by someone doing other stuff. I LIKE that tension.
e) Too fiddly. Too much moving components do distract from what makes a game good. Simple, elegant, slick design. Deep Sea Adventure. Onitama. El Grande. Heck, even Dominion. All examples of games that just focus on one mechanic and do it great. Less is more people. That holds true for games as well.

Now, if you like point-salad, broad action space games with lots of moving parts, have a lot of time to invest in a game, don't own many games already and are not interested in theme (or happen to dig the 'real cowboy' theme), this might be totally up your ally. 

Not my ally.

RATING: 10
tl;dr: more of the same... good stuff!

This game was hyped at the show after last year's [thing=172386][/thing] turned out so well. It went on my radar but the western theme doesn't help. But it was clear this is a traditional, rather heavy euro, not a real cowboy gunslinger game. Good.
First, let me say that the rules where very well written and structured. I had to do very little lookup work even though there are quite some rules tied to a couple of actions or locations. It does have a lot of nested 'if' statements. If you do this action, then you check that. If that is the case, then the following might occur. If the result of that is B, than  something else might happen. That kind of thing. But we had few questions and found the answers easily. And when playing, everything does make sense.
Anyway: this game is not original. Build some buildings, use some building's actions, use resources to score points... All stuff we've seen before, but it's all put together skilfully. Every turn you have to keep in mind a good number of variables and make plans for the future while still staying flexible.
I must say I really enjoyed playing this game a lot. It looks like Pfister has an other hit on his resume. Sweet.

Initial rating: 7.5/10
BGG scale: 8/10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Point salad, but with a nice framework. Cowboys cycle through a branching-path network, starting in Texas and ending in Kansas City. Elements in the game include: a Dominion-like draw of cattle; areas along the network which allow building; a mostly linear train track, VP-collector. Cowboys let you buy more and cheaper cattle; artisans allow higher level buildings, which only the owning player can use and may impose a passing penalty on others; and engineers (magnates?--they wear top hats) that move your train faster.

I tend to think cattle/cowboys are needed. Whether buildings/artisans or train/engineers are superior or need balancing, I'm unsure. More plays needed.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2.5
I'm going to give this game the most backhanded compliment I've ever given: this is likely the best terrible game I've ever played.

It's terrible because it's incredibly overwrought, which adds immense breadth but little depth.  This breadth makes the game a complete slog to teach (40 minutes from a great teacher!) but then is at heart an incredibly simple game.  This is the purest example of everything AS WELL AS the kitchen sink that I can think of.

It's terrible because the game can take almost three hours but there is never once a feeling of ebb and flow or drama because it is almost impossible to tell who is in the lead at any given time.  Because EVERYTHING gets you victory points.  So whose multitude of victory points are highest at any given time?  *shrug*  Though I guess is doesn't really matter because there's nothing you can do to really affect other players, which brings me to...

It's terrible because I've played few other games that offer so little player interaction.  This is multiplayer solitaire at its worst.  You could literally play the entire game with only a rare glance at what other people are doing and do just fine.  That's forgivable in a game that takes 60 minutes or less but not so here.

It's terrible because I was excited to play a game with this unique theme of driving cattle but the theme just does not come through.  This is how the players talk during the game: "Ok, I move two spaces.  I'm going to stop here and convert this black card into two coins and my second action allows me to do an auxiliary action, so I'll draw two cards... *draws* and discard these two." YEEHAAW!

The most frustrating thing is that I can see sparkles of a great game nestling within this steaming cow patty.  This could have been a fun family game if the designer or publisher had just said "whoa!" and reined things in.  As is, though, I can't imagine this game having much longevity.  There's a reason why the Knizia classics had legs. Modern game design needs to look back a little further for its influences.  To me, Great Western Trail is the epitome of what is wrong with post-Agricola Euro games.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Move along the path to Kansas City, selling your cattle, diversifying your herd, improving your business operations, and ultimately making money to do it all again. Buildings you build on the path either give you better actions, or additional money since opponents passing through owe you a fee. Throw in a subtle deck-building element and you've got a unique-feeling, heavy-weight, Pfister Euro. I wish it played just a bit shorter, but aside from that, it's a solid design.

I give it an 8 after two plays. But I need more to decide if it's a keeper. In my first two games, the same strategy was the winning strategy. We'll see.

::UPDATE::

My third play proves there are more strategies that the one I saw employed by the winners in the first two games I played. I also think the winning strategy is always going to be dependent on how each game develops, which is a really good thing. I like it when the players determine the mood of the game. I'm still keeping my rating at 8, but I'm starting to think I'll be keeping it. Each time I play, I find myself grinding the gears on what I could have done better. Can't wait to play more.

::UPDATE::

And my fourth play bumps it up from an 8 to a solid 9. This is a really good game. It may end up among my favorites if it continues to show more and more layers. Well done, Pfister!

RATING: 7
Great Western Trail is what I call a "Kitchen sink" game, where the complexity of the game arises from throwing a ton of mechanisms and stuff at you. Initially it is pretty overwhelming but about halfway through the game things started making sense and it felt a lot simpler than it originally looked.

The game is a deckbuilding game that doesn't feel like a deckbuilding game. You start with a deck of cow cards which are different colors and values. You'll be moving your pawn along paths on the board eventualy reaching Kansas city at the end of the track.

Once you hit Kansas city, you present your hand and total up all the cow values in your hand, but you can only use one card of each color. Then you remove a disc from your player board (which gives you some bonus) and put it in a city spot that equals or exceeds your "cow score". You can only visit most cities once, so you'll need better and better cow scores as the game goes along. There's always a spot at the 0 cow score spot, but you lose 6 points for going there. There's also bonuses and penalties for taking two adjacent city spots (bonuses for the high cities, penalties for the low cities). Additionally at Kansas city, you get a little bit of choice about seeding the board with new stuff and you get money equal to your cow score.

When you move around the board, you must follow a path. You have a base movement rate which is how many buildings you can pass or get to on your turn. So if your movement is 3, you can move skipping over two buildings, then skipping over two hazard and landing on the next building. You must pay a buck or two penalty for passing a hazard. When you land on a building, you can trigger its power assuming it is either your building or a public building. You get a compensation for landing on another person's building, but don't do that. The game starts with long paths without many buildings... but as the game goes along players can building buildings, making it harder to get far on the paths, and giving themselves more building abilities to trigger.

Buildings let you do various things, like hire helpers, play certain cow cards to get money, buy new cards from a public offering, build your personal buildings, etc. Your buit buildings give you points at the end of the game. Some buildings also count as hazards for your opponents. Some of them push you further along the track when you land on them.

There's also a train track that you're trying to advance on. Essentially you pay a money penalty when you hit Kansas City if you try to score a large city, but you're not far enough on the train track. There's also stations along the train path, where you get to remove discs from your player board and get points... and possibly get a scoring bonus goal.

You also start the game with a contract you're trying to fill for bonus points. There's way to get more contracts, from a public display, and a way that you're forced to sometimes. New contracts go into your discard pile and clog your deck. You get a bonus for playing a contract, but then you're committed to completing it and there is a penalty for failing to. Contracts are mostly "Do these 3 things in the game and get X points" type of stuff, so if you take contracts that synergize with stuff you're already doing, it's free points.

There's a lot going on in GWT. I had a lot of fun... once I started getting the game. I do have a concern that the game might have balance issues. I'm not sure if all the strategies are balanced. Also, it's a bit heavier than most deckbuilders, so random draws going against you is more painful than they are in a typical deckbuilder.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
[thing=193738][/thing] is an intense mix: hand management, deck building, movement, tile placement. The feel while playing it was a profane match between [thing=18602][/thing] (due to the order in which to acctivate the buildings, which demands a lot of planning beforehand) and [thing=58421][/thing] (due to, once a place is passed, there is no way to return to it in the same travel).

Yet, it is a mix that works: the mechanics are clever, there is a fine dispute for places in the train track, for positioning the buildings in the path, for hiring workers, etc; though most of it is indirect. The playing time can grow fast, since there is so many ways to go and choices to make, still the pacing of the game gains a lot if players think about what they want to do before is their turn, since the others usually (not always) interfere little with your next move.

[thing=193738][/thing] gives a big amount of choices and options to the players, but this comes at a price: the already mentioned playing time (which can get near, of even pass, 3 hours), and the long and tedious explaining of the rules, as there are many details spread over several parts of the game, all with relevant connections with other parts, thus needing explaining. The learning curve is also rather steep.

[thing=193738][/thing] offers a multitude of paths to follow (both literally, going on by the buildings on the board; as figurativelly, as in strategies to win), lots of timming concerns, considerations about positioning of buildings, purchases of cattle, advancements on the train track, and so on. It also has a good replay value, due to the random positioning of the starting buildings, the blind draw of hazards and workers, and even the use of the A and B sides of the players buildings.

Overall, [thing=193738][/thing] puts itself as a deep and good euro, one that definitely won't fit everyone tastes, considering the high price tag for entering and staying: the rules (specially for those constantly playing with new people needing to learn) and the demands of planning in long term in a long game. Still, by now, you probably know if [thing=193738][/thing] is for you or not.

RATING: 7
GWT will undoubtedly be compared to Mombasa due to Pfister's back to back award winning designs in as many years. Both these games spot very different mechanisms with Mombasa having the refreshing deck recycling mechanism while GWT has the more ubiquitous deck building system. After initial plays, Mombasa just feels more fun, involved, interactive and less repetitive than GWT. GWT however is ranked more highly among gamers and I can see why.

In GWT, turn order is rapid. Something you would not expect from a game with mid to high level of complexity. Folks move their cowboy meeples along the trail and perform a single action. That's it. Turns often fly by and before you can gather your thoughts, your turn is up again.  Generally, fast-paced games with rapid turns is a thumbs up for me. In GWT, I just had trouble absorbing all the information between turns. Consequently, I lost track of player progress and the game became a multi-player solitaire to a certain extent. Turns went by quickly and new buildings were erected, new guys appeared on the job board and were hired, etc. All the while, it was tough incorporating those changes into my own strategy. That, I found somewhat exasperating. I can generally think on my feet, but for some reason, the game quickly became very individual as players just went about their business with low frequency interaction. Even the interactions themselves weren't exactly quality interactions. I myself dislike fiddly rules of "pay-as-you-use" or "pay-if-you-pass" type mechanisms ala Caylus. They just make the game feel busy in a bad way. Eventually, in the late game stages, the pace and rhythm does pick up as the supply of money becomes more plentiful and actions can be strung out to develop the engine. It is neat to build the economic engine, but when things are finally chugging smoothly, the game winds down. My high-value cows never had a chance to go through the deck.

Components wise, the game is fine. Pieces are great but auxiliary player mats are thin. The tracks on the board can be tough to follow and you can lose your path at times. 

The game for me is interesting and demands further exploration. It is a solid Euro with a mix of mechanisms just like Mombasa, but the interlocking pieces in GWT, I feel is not nearly as coherent as Mombasa. Nonetheless, a very good follow up from Pfister and certainly one that will win many accolades in the upcoming months (Kennerspiel perhaps?)

RATING: 9
sleeves

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Alexander Pfister is on an incredible roll. We haven't seen a run like this since Stephan Feld's 2007-2013 stretch, where every game seemed to be better than the last, with all of them being worthy of a greatest hits compilation. Great Western Trail is easily the best thing to come out of Essen 2016.

The game is sort of like Caylus, but with a deck building aspect. Players drive cattle from Texas to Kansas City, attempting to arrive at their destination with the highest value of collected cattle cards in their hand. The catch is that any duplicate cards are not counted. So the primary focus of each cattle run is to find a way to discard duplicate cards while at the same time adding higher valued cattle cards to your deck.

The Caylus vibe comes from the trail itself. It is a forking pathway that starts the game largely empty, but as the game moves on, becomes cluttered with player erected building spaces that both slow and tax anyone that passes over them. This makes every game unique but not random, since how each session develops is completely controlled by the player's actions.

The railroad aspect of the game feels a little tacked on, but I suppose it was something that was added in development, to soak up some money and keep things tight. Regardless, it works and gives everyone one more thing to think about when income from the cattle drive is collected, making that phase something more interesting than a simple clean-up phase.

The learning curve is a little steep for the first game, but once players have been through a full cattle drive, most of it comes together pretty well. I'd say it's about on par with Mombasa, as far as complexity goes. A mouthful to teach, but simple in turn to turn execution. Good stuff.

RATING: N/A
Driving cattle to Kansas City along a route with area movement and tile placement of buildings and obstacles with final sale and delivery by rail to the outlying cities in the Midwest.  Player boards have unlocks to enhance basic actions.  Hire Cowboys, craftsmen and engineers from the labor market that doubles as a game end countdown timer.  Set collection from identical cattle decks to build big cattle sale hands without duplicates.  Thin out the common low value jersey cow cards and add high value chocolate and purple cows for bigger sales.  A big part of the game is tile placement of your buildings in other rancher paths and handling obstacles and Indians on the trail.  There is a lot going on in this one with lots of tactical revisions.

RATING: 6.9
It is an ok Euro game. I love the theme though. 

GWT plays a bit like monopoly. Think about it. You go around the board, stopping off at locations and taking actions which require money. In certain places where an opponent has built an building or if there is a hazard, you must either pay your opponent or the bank.

Initially, it is hard to read the rules and translate what you have read into playing the game. There are some good video tutorials available which really help one's ability to digest the rules. There is a learning curve as well. Learning what each tile does will take a little bit of practice. With that said once you begin playing the game, it plays quite fluidly. The tiles will also become intuitive.

My favorite parts of the game are hiring workers, going to the cattle market and the train movement/Kansas city mechanism. Really unique and I enjoy these actions. They make it a good game.

However, the game is mostly a single-player solitaire - waiting for your turn Euro. In the game, there is minimal interaction or anything else to stir the pot of Cookie's beans. The game needs more excitement. Why not have tiles like Bad Bart's Hideout where cattle rustlers live or a branding/cattle count location which have good and negative effects on all players on the trail if the tile is played in the game, (in other words the actions one player takes on the tile affects his competitors, but if he does so, he also has to pay a price)? Why not add random cards mixed in with the achievement cards like stampede, flooding, or hostile Indians which affect people in certain pats of the trail? it might make the achievement cards more interesting. While the achievement cards are means for victory, at the same time the general feeling is that you don't want them because they crowd up your deck. Ideas like this would add more chili to Cookie's beans, thus making the cattle punchers farts that much stronger and essentially the game much more interesting. I am surprised things like cattle counting and/or cattle rustlers. flooding, hostile Indians (etc.) were left out of the game. These events happened and always make the plot for the best western movies! Indeed, there are hazards in the game, but they are simply avoided until it becomes more expensive not to avoid them by going the another way. Would interactivity make all of the passive-aggressive snowflake Euro gamers cry? Probably. For me, the lack of interaction is a major drawback and why the game does not deserve a higher rating. 

GWT is way overrated at 8.3.  I love western games. Very few games use the theme and then implement it well. The theme here is excellent. However, GWT will never replace Carson City. It does come in at a much lower second though. Carson City remains in my top 10.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
While the rulebook was difficult to follow (I recommend watching a playthrough before reading), the game itself is one of those "easy to play, hard to master" type games. The iconography is EXCELLENT -- while there are many actions you may take, they all use the same "pieces" to assemble the icon and tell you what each space does. Even in the first games, I very rarely had to reference the rulebook because everything you need to know is present in the icons. The gameplay is a neat mix of light deckbuilding, hand management, and sort of a worker placement mechanism -- you decide which space(s) you want to stop on, as long as you're following the path and not exceeding your movement limit. Wonderful design.

RATING: 7
I'm not [b]entirely[/b] sure how I feel about this yet, but I like it. Let me explain: The first time I played, my delightful opponents were (as one often is with a game like this) too absorbed with their strategies, tactics, and schemes to notice that I was erroneously [b]paying[/b] the amount of the chosen station at the end of the trail each time, and thus was always destitute. Ouch. I had an abysmal score. So, I taught someone else how to play the game — correctly, I believe — and I got spanked around the block, and ended with an even worse score. :laugh:

I also have concerns about the intentional bottleneck at the first turn on the trail, but I will just have to play it more, because [b]there is so much awesome about this game[/b]. Oh, except the meticulous sluice of prospective employees; that really feels like a lot more work than it needs to be for what it gives the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
This game has a really bad complexity / fun ratio. You need to study the rulebook, rather than just read it, and even then it takes several plays before you no longer forget any detail rules. You need to set up an “engine”, but it’s really hard to get it rolling. One element that is really baked into the game is that you have to scoop the other players: snatch away the station tiles, get the best cattle cards, occupy the most favorable spots for your building tiles, grab the cheapest person tiles, get the best disaster tiles etc.  It can be somewhat frustrating if you feel that the scooping among players is out of balance. Finally, there is no score track that is updated during the game, so that it’s difficult to get a feeling how well you’re doing compared to the other players.
